==PAGE:1==
Game Based Control Program for Railway
Networks
Master Thesis
Matias Daugaard Holst-Christensen
s153198
August 2020
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
In this project a simple railway model will be explored, to examine ways to
create correct-by-construction control programs for the railway networks. This
will be done by looking at the problem as a two player game, with a Controller
in charge of signals and switch-rails, and a Conductor in charge of moving the
trains as the two players. A control program can then be found by creating a
game graph over the game, and ﬁnding a path, which ensures that the Controller
will always win, no matter how the Conductor acts. To avoid having to generate
the entire game graph, which size grows exponentially, on-the-ﬂy generating of
states will be used.
In extension to this the trains will before the game be
ordered and have their paths calculated on order to further reduce the number
of states needed to be generated. This will be solved as a single agent problem
at ﬁrst. Later we will look into merging the single agent solution into multi
agent solutions, as a way to make the solutions more eﬃcient and shorter. In
the project a graphical user interface, to create and editor railway networks and
to showcase the found solutions, has also been implemented.
1

Chapter 1
Introduction
In this project we will explore a simple railway setting, to model networks and
create correct-by-constructing control programs for the signals and switch-rail,
a similar idea also explored by [1][3]. The point of this program is to guide
the trains from one location to another in a safe manner. Meaning that even
if the trains do not move in the intended or optimal way, the control program
will ensure that the trains will arrive at their goals, and without any of them
crashing into one another. This is as long as the trains do not break any rules,
such as moving past a red signal. The way this will be done is by looking at
the problem as a two-player game. Player 1 being the Controller, which has
the ability to change the signals between red and green, and change switch-rails
between the rail options. And player 2 is a Conductor who can move the trains
on the railway, if allowed and possible by the rails, signals and switch-rails.
The way the game is played is with the Controller starting by changing
signals color or switch-rails options or by doing nothing.
When all wanted
actions have been performed the turn is given to the Conductor. This player
can move then the as many trains as wanted by one rail in this new setting with
the changes from the Controller. And the turn is then returned to the Controller.
The game then continues in this manner and if all the trains end up in their goal
location the Controller will have won the game. Which means a control program
has been found, otherwise the Conductor has won. The control program, will
then be the actions the Controller took, when trains where at certain locations,
to guide the trains to their goals.
To be a correct control program, it also
needs to ensure that even if the Conductor has acted diﬀerently at some point,
the Controller would still have won the game. Meaning that no matter how
the Conductor plays the game, the Controller will always win. The Conductor
could win by never moving the trains, and in extension therefore never have
them reach their goal. So to avoid this the Conductor has to move at least one
train if possible. By looking at the problem of creating a control program this
way as a game, it will be possible to create a game graph. Where the states or
nodes are the current game board, which includes the location of the trains, color
of the signals and selected switch-rail option, and the edges being the actions
5
==PAGE:7==
performed by the players. This means that it is possible to simulate the game
for a network and create the game graph. This can then be searched through
to ﬁnd a way that ensures the Controller will win no matter what, or in other
words ﬁnd a correct control program. However some problems arise. Because
the game graph grows exponentially in the size of the network. In fact there
exists P ∗2#Signal+#Switch−rail states, where P = Q#L
n=#L−#T rain(n), and #L is
number of locations the trains can be. Not all of these are possible, as trains
in this setting are only allowed to move in one direction, and thereby cannot
reach all locations. But this shows that the number of states quickly becomes
impossible to work with. So ﬁnding a winning strategy for the Controller would
be both time and memory consuming if even possible at all. That means that
creating the entire game graph is not really an option, if the railway have a
certain size. Instead we will look at a more dynamic approach using on-the-
ﬂy generating of the states.
In this the states will only be generated when
needed, meaning we might only need to create a fraction of the game graph, as
we only need to ﬁnd one way to get the trains to goal and not all. However
this can still result in creating a lot of states, as the Controller could generate
up to 2#Signal+#Switch−rail states each turn, and the Conductor up to 2#T rain
states. In this project some measures to reduce this number will be explored and
implemented. Which include looking at the problem as a single agent problem,
only allowing one train to move at a time, and prioritizing and path-ﬁnding of
the trains. This is all done as to try to ﬁnd a correct control program as eﬃcient
as possible, meaning generating as few states as possible, to reduce the amount
of time and memory needed.
In this project we will also try to ﬁnd an eﬃcient control program. One
thing is ﬁnding a solution eﬃciently, but the solution itself might not eﬃcient.
An eﬃcient solution, is a control program which will guide the trains to their
goals as fast as possible. Meaning that the total number of states needed to get
from the start to the goal is as low as possible. To achieve this we will look into
a multi agent solution. As mentioned one of the ideas to reduce the number of
states generated was to solve the problem single agently. Because one of the
big problems with multi agent problems, is that it grows exponentially in the
number of agents or here trains. Meaning one of the best measures taken to
reduce the state generating will be useless. To avoid this, the approach taken for
a multi agent solution will be merging a single agent solution for a network into
a multi agent solution. A similar idea explored in [4]. This means we can use
the eﬃciently found single agent control program, to create an eﬃcient multi
agent solution. So we will not have to compromise between the two, and can
create an eﬃcient control program eﬃciently. If the merging is also performed
eﬃcient.
The second part of the project is to create a graphical user interface. This
will be used to both create and modify railway networks in a simple and easy
way, and to showcase networks and the control programs found. Creating a GUI
is also to avoid typing errors, when creating networks, by having a program
automatically translate what the railway looks like into a format the solver
program can read. It can also help visualize what the control program looks
6
==PAGE:8==
like, and help ﬁnd unexpected behaviour, which would otherwise be hard to
spot.
The point of a program like this is not to create a timetable for the trains,
but get the trains safely past a problematic part of a railway, if something has
gone wrong. Lets say one of the rail has been damaged by a falling tree and
is no longer usable. How should the trains go now, since the original plan no
longer works. This is the situation a program like this could be used. The new
situation could be modelled and the solver program would automatically create
a safe plan, to get the trains past the problematic rail, and from there the normal
plan could take over again. Problems like this arise very often, due to many
diﬀerent thing, such as technical error or weather, and having an automated
way to ﬁnd a solution would be very nice, and could make the process faster
and smarter.
The focus of the project will be the implementation of the control program,
which will be done using the programming language F#, and the implementa-
tion of the graphical user interface using Java.
7
==PAGE:9==
Chapter 2
The Railway Domain
Before we start looking for a solution to the problem, we need to precise the
domain. Such that we know, what is allowed and what is not. Let us ﬁrst take a
look at the elements we need in the railway setting. First up we will need some
trains and some locations they can stay. We then need some way of connecting
these locations, which are the rails. For the control program to actually be able
to do anything we also need signals. Such that we can tell the trains if they are
allowed to move or not in some direction. And if the railways should not only
consists of single lines, there should also be some switch-rails, which is a rail
that can go to two locations but only one at a time. These are the gonna be the
basis for all of the networks we are gonna be looking at in this project. This is
a very simplistic version of a real railway, as there are numerous diﬀerent kinds
of signals and switch-rails in real railways. But to avoid being overwhelmed
and going too much into all the speciﬁcs of all of those, we will use this simple
model. So lets look at how we deﬁne each of them.
8
==PAGE:10==
Figure 2.1: Figure showing a simple railway. In circle a, we see a train at a
location (red dot) going right. The location also contains two signals, the red
for going left and the green for going right from the location. In circle b, we see a
single rail connecting two locations. In circle c we see a left directed switch-rail,
currently being able to use the lower rail. The bottom left location has a signal
for going right, and the bottom right location has a signal for going left.
The locations will each be deﬁned by a unique number. We can then deﬁne
the rails as a tuple consisting of two locations. That way we can easily create
a graph over the network with the locations as the nodes and the rails as the
edges. This will be an undirected graph as it is usually possible to go both
directions on a rail, or at least it is in this model. By creating such a graph
we can make use of graph searching algorithm for ﬁnding a way for a train to
move from one location to another, which will be very helpful, when trying to
guide them. To follow the way real railways work, we will only allow a location
to be connected by at most two rail (or one rail and a switch-rail). That way
leaving a location in one direction will always take you to the same location, if
it is connected by a normal rail. The next type is the signal. These are used for
telling if a train is allowed to move from one location in a direction at a given
time. This means that we need to deﬁne the signals as having a location and
a direction. Because it might only be leaving a location to the left that needs
a signal. Finally we have the switch-rails. These are a kind of rail that allow a
location to have two rail leaving in the same direction, but with only one being
allowed at a time. So when leaving a location which is connected to a switch-
rail, the switch-rail setting will decide which location is reached. This means
that we need to know at which location this switch-rail is, which two locations it
is connected to and the direction of the switch-rail, see ﬁgure Figure 2.1. Only
the selected rail of a switch-rail is usable even if going in the opposite direction.
These are the basic things need for a network, however one thing is missing in
order to solve a problem, there needs to be one. The problem we wish to solve
is to ﬁnd a way to guide trains from one location to another. This means that
we need some trains, and we need to know the starting location of a train and
the goal location. As a mean to simplify the problem, trains are only allowed to
move in one direction. This will reduce the number of locations a train can be
9
==PAGE:11==
and will allow for some reduction in search space and time later when looking
for a solution.
Symbol
Description
D
Set of directions. Consists of Left and Right.
L
List of locations. Each element l ∈L is a unique integer.
R
List of rails. Each element r ∈R is deﬁned a tuple (l1, l2),
where l1 ∈L and l2 ∈L.
SR
List of switch-rails. Each element sr ∈SR is deﬁned as a
quadruple (l1, l2, l3, d), where l1 ∈L, l2 ∈L, l3 ∈L
and d ∈D. l1 is connecting to l2 and l3 in direction d
S
List of signals. Each element s ∈S is deﬁned as a tuple
(l, d), where l ∈L and d ∈D. As having a signal moving
from l in direction d.
T
List of trains. Each element t ∈T is deﬁned as a quadruple
(i, l1, l2, d), where i is a unique string identifying the train,
l2 ∈L, l3 ∈L and d ∈D. l1 is the starting location,
l2 is the goal location and d is the direction of the train.
RN
The railway network. Deﬁned as the quintuple (L,R,SR,S,T).
Table 2.1: Deﬁnitions of the railway domain.
With all this information we can now deﬁne a railway network as being a
quintuple consisting of a list of locations, a list of rail, a list of switch-rails, a
list of signals and a list of trains, with their start and goal locations and their
direction see Table 2.1.
There has already been mentioned a few restrictions, which has been put on
the railway model we are gonna use. First was that a location can at most be
connected to two rails, or a rail and and a switch-rail. So a location cannot be
connected to two switch-rails. Second was that no matter the direction of the
train, only the selected rail of a switch-rail can be used. And ﬁnally that trains
are only allowed to go in one direction, which means no going in reverse. These
are all put in place to both reduce the amount of states which can exist and to
make the railways more easy to keep track of and realistic. There are however
a few more things that needs to be restricted. The ﬁrst is a consequence of
trains only being able to go in one direction, which is that there has to be a
path from the starting location to the goal location of all trains in the network.
This is fairly obvious as if this does not exists a the trains cannot arrive at
their goals. Then the game cannot be won by the Controller, which means a
control program does not exists. The next is that the starting location of a train
should always have a signal for leaving in the same direction as the direction
of the train. This is just in place to keep any trains from moving without the
consent of the Controller, which could lead to problems that are not solvable.
Because the trains can crash into each other without the Controller being able
to do anything. Following along this line the goal location of a train should also
always have signal going in the same direction as the train, or be a dead end.
10
==PAGE:12==
Meaning that no rail is leaving the location in the direction of the train. This is
to make sure that the train can be stopped at the goal location, otherwise the
Controller cannot ensure that all the trains will be in goal at the same time,
meaning a correct control program does not exists. The next restriction is also a
fairly obvious rule. A location can at most be the starting location of one train,
and the same for goal locations. Again otherwise the problem will be unsolvable
as more than one train has to occupy the same location as once, which means
the trains will have crashed, and no solution exists. Finally a switch-rail should
always have a signal on the location intersecting the two rail options in the
direction of the switch-rail.
And on the other two locations in the opposite
direction.
This rule is to make it seem realistic as there usually are signals
close to the switch-rails, as these are places things could go very wrong. For an
example of a railway see Figure 2.2. This models the railway from Østerport
Station to Copenhagen Central Station, without the S-train part1.
Figure 2.2: An example of a railway network. This has been modelled after the
railway going from the left Østerport Station to the right Copenhagen Central
Station. The red line are the rails, the L and R symbolizes a signal at a location
in direction left or right. The upper case T is the trains starting location and
lowercase t is the goal location, for a given number. Train placement is randomly
picked.
As mentioned the way we are gonna look at this problem is by considering
1The model is based on information found on OpenRailwayMap[6]. The signals have been
added to follow the domain rules, and long stretches have been shortened
11
==PAGE:13==
it as a two player game, with the Controller having the ability to control the
signals and switch-rails.
And the Conductor being able to move the trains.
That way we can create a game graph over the states these are generating, and
and see if we can ﬁnd a path, which will end up with all the trains being placed
at their goal locations. But before we can do that we need to deﬁne what a state
is, how we can get from one to another and what the game graph looks like. As
mentioned the Controller can change the signals and the switch-rails, and the
Conductor can move the train. This means that the only things that can change
between states are the signals color, switch-rails rails and trains locations. So
for each state we will only need to keep track of whether or not each signal is
green, for each switch-rail which of the two rails are currently selected and for
the trains where they are on the railway. We can therefore deﬁne a state as a
triple consisting of a map mapping each signal to a value, to indicate whether
the signal is green or red. A map mapping each switch-rail to a value, to indicate
which of the two rail options are selected, and a map mapping each train to a
location, see Table 2.2.
Symbol
Description
SM
Signal map. Key being a signal. Value whether or not it is green.
SRM
Switch-rail map. Key being a switch-rail. Value which of the
rail options are selected.
TM
Train map. Key being a train id. Value the location of the train.
S
State. Deﬁned as the triple (SM,SRM,TM).
Table 2.2: The deﬁnition of the state in the game.
Now we have deﬁned the state, we can look at how we can generate them,
move between them and create the game graph. First we will need to deﬁne the
initial state. This one is rather straightforward as this will just be the state,
where all the signals are red. The switch-rails being one of the two options
chosen, which does not matter, as the Controller always can change it before
any train can reach it. And the trains will be placed at their starting positions
given by the railway network. As stated earlier all trains have to be placed at a
location with a signal. This along with the fact that all signals are red, means
that the Conductor cannot do anything from the initial state. This is what we
wanted, to ensure that the Controller always have a chance to ﬁnd a solution if
it exists. Secondly we need to deﬁne the goal state. The goal state will be the
state in which all of the trains are located at their goal location. This means
that the value of the signals and switch-rail are not relevant, and that there
therefore are multiple states which qualify as being the goal state, and a more
accurate name would therefore be the goal states. So the goal states is the set
of states in which all trains are at their goal location.
Now how do we then get from the initial state to one of the goal states. We
will explain this by looking at a simple example, with a network containing two
trains, a switch-rail and the needed signals, see Figure 2.3.
12
==PAGE:14==
Figure 2.3: The left showing the initial state, and the right showing a goal state,
of a simple railway network.
As mentioned we will not generate all game states, and just search through
them for a path, as the number of states quickly becomes impossible to work
with. Instead to get from the initial state to a goal state we need to generate
the states that are in between in the game graph.
This is what is done by
the two players. The Controller can generate new states by changing signal or
switch-rail values, and the Conductor by changing the location of the trains.
There are however a few rules for the Conductor. Trains can only be move to
a new location if the old is connected to the new one by a rail or switch-rail
in the direction of the train. And if they are connected by at switch-rail, the
rail has to be the selected option. Trains can also only move from a location
if a signal green or no signal is present for the direction. As mentioned earlier
the Conductor cannot do anything from the initial state, as the red light are
blocking all the trains from going.
This means that the only one that can
generate states at ﬁrst is the Controller. The Controller can change as many
signal and switch-rail values as it want to, meaning one way to generate states
is to ﬁnd all combination of signal and switch-rail values and create them. All
of these will be legal states, which are reachable from the initial state by a
single step, however most of them will not be relevant, as they do not allow
any of the trains to move. If we look at Figure 2.4, some of the states from
the example network initial state which has been generated by the Controller
has been illustrated. They can be seen as the three states at the end of the
arrow from the initial state at the top left. It is now the Conductors turn. He
can move any train he wants along one rail. As we can see from the ﬁgure two
of the states, will end up with trains being at the same location and therefore
means that the Controller has lost the game, as the trains have crashed. We
do however have one new state which is still good, the top right. From here
the Controller again has many options for state generation, but only one which
allows for train movement can be seen. From this new state the Conductor only
has one option which is to move the train down left, which moves it to its goal.
13
==PAGE:15==
This means all the train are now in goal and a goal state has been found. We
can then backtrack from the goal state to ﬁnd a path in the game graph which
leads to a solution, by following the arrows backwards.
Figure 2.4: Part of the game graph, for a simple railway network. Top left
showing the initial state and a goal state in the bottom right.
The arrows
indicate which player has made a move, to go from one state to the other. The
bottom left and middle states, showing states where the Conductor has won by
the two trains crashing into each other. The route from the initial state to a
goal state, showcase by following the arrows right and then down. The ﬁgure
only shows some of the states, as many of the irrelevant has been removed.
However one thing is ﬁnding a solution, another thing is ﬁnding a correct
solution. So what makes a solution a correct solution. A correct solution is a
solution where all of the states will end up in a goal state no matter what the
Conductor decides to do, of legal actions. In the shown example the solution is
a correct solution as the Conductor only has one option in each of the states,
which is to move a single train. Not moving it is also an option, but would lead
to the same state, and therefore is not counted, also as mentioned the Conductor
14
==PAGE:16==
has to move a train if possible otherwise the train will never arrive at goals. This
means all the states in the solution leads to a goal state. However if we look at
another example in Figure 2.5, we see that by following the arrow down, then
right then down and down we get to a goal state, so we have found a solution.
But the problem here is that in the state generated by the Controller ﬁrst, the
middle left, the Conductor has multiple options. The one shown by following
the arrow down, leads to the two trains being on the same location by moving
the left most train one to the right, and not moving the other. This crashes
the trains and loses the game for the Controller. This is therefore not a correct
solution as this state, which was part of the found solution, does not always
lead to a goal state. This means that we need to make sure, when we look for
a solution all of the states the Conductor can generate, from any state in the
solution has to lead to a goal state, not just one of them, for it to be a correct
solution.
Figure 2.5: Part of the game graph, for a railway network. Top left state is
initial state, and bottom right is a goal state. This is an example of a non-
correct solution, as one of the states in the solution allows for the Conductor to
crash the trains, meaning the Controller lost the game.
15
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 3
The Basic Setup
In this section the basic setup of the code and data-ﬁles will be described.
Before we start coding the solver we ﬁrst need to setup some basic types, data-
structures and design ﬁle types for the railways and solutions, such that they
can easily be stored and loaded when needed.
Lets start by looking at the ﬁles types. We will need two diﬀerent types of
ﬁles. One for storing railway networks, and one for storing the solutions. For
storing the railways we will go with a simple .txt ﬁle, that has to be setup in
a certain way. The order of the ﬁle will be the following, ﬁrst locations, then
rails, switch-rail, signals, trains and ﬁnally the dimensions of the railway, height
and width needed for the GUI. Each of these will be sectioned up separated
by a colon (:) on a line by itself. Each element of the network will be written
on its own line, and follows the description seen in Table 2.1.
For the ﬁrst
section the locations, the number representing the location will be written, the
order of locations is irrelevant. For the rail section, the two locations the rail
is connecting will be on the same line separated by a space. The order of the
locations the rail is consisting of will not be relevant, and can therefore be either
left to right or the other way. Each switch-rail will require four parts, ﬁrst the
three locations and then the direction, these has to be separated by a space.
The order of the locations is very important here. The ﬁrst location should be
the one that intersects the switch-rail. The second the one on the same row as
the intersecting one, the GUI editor always requires one of the rail options to
go along the row of the intersecting rail. And then at last the third location
for the other rail option. The direction has to be marked as either L or R, for
left or right, and is the direction of the switch-rail, an example of a left directed
switch-rail can be seen in circle c in Figure 2.1, where the intersecting location
is the bottom right location also in circle c. Next up is the signals, these will
be described by their location and their direction, separated by a space. The
direction is again either L or R. We then have the trains, these have four parts
also. The unique id of the train, the starting location, the end location and the
direction, these are as well also separated by spaces. Finally we have the width,
and the height. These are two integers each on their own line, and is used only
16
==PAGE:18==
by the GUI, to set up the correct size grid. For an example see Figure 3.1
Figure 3.1: An example of a railway network ﬁle. This is the one for the railway
network seen in Figure 2.5.
For the solution ﬁles, we will use a ﬁle type called .sol. The reason for not
using a .txt as with the railway networks, is that they can then be named to
same, making it easy to pair them together. The solution ﬁle is split up into
sections each containing a single state of the solution, in order of initial state
being the ﬁrst and the ﬁnal state being last in the ﬁle. These sections are each
surrounded by a token pair of <> and < / > to symbolize the start and end of a
state. Each state then contains three lines. The ﬁrst is started by a < trains >
token followed by a semicolon separated list of the locations of the trains, in
alphabetical order of the train ids. The second line is the signals values in the
state. This is started by the < signals > token and followed by a list of Ts and
Fs, short for true and false. The T meaning a signal is green in the state and
F meaning it is red. The order of the list is that the ﬁrst element is the signal
with the smallest location value and the last with the largest. If two signals
share a location the one with direction left is ﬁrst in the list. The last line is the
switch-rails started by a < rails > token. Following this is a also a semicolon
separated list of Ts and Fs. In this case the T is for the rail, which is on the
same row as the intersecting location being selected and the F for the other.
The list is ordered such that the ﬁrst element is the switch-rail with the smallest
location of the intersecting location part of the switch-rail, and the last element
being the one with the largest value. After all the states in the ﬁle, three more
17
==PAGE:19==
lines exists.
The ﬁrst has the token < states > followed by the number of
states in the solution. The second starts with the token < generated >. This is
followed by the number of states, that where generated when trying to ﬁnd the
solution. And ﬁnally an line started by the token < time >. This line is the
total time spent ﬁnding a solution in millisecond. The entire ﬁles is wrapped
by the tokens < solution > and < /solution >, which serves no purpose. An
example of a solution ﬁle can be seen in Figure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: An example of a solution ﬁle. This is the one for the solution to the
railway network seen in Figure 3.1
Next up we will look at the data types needed. The solver will be written
using F# and we will therefore make use of the easy way to create types in
F#. The types needed are the ones described in Table 2.1 and Table 2.2. Lets
look at the ones from the domain ﬁrst. These can all be seen in Figure 3.3, and
are mostly translated directly from the tables. The few things that are a bit
18
==PAGE:20==
diﬀerent is that the direction type is deﬁned as L or R. This is just to shorten
left and right. But lets go through them one by one. First up is the Train, this
is a string used to identify the trains. Next is the Direction, can be either L or
R, and is used for various element to signify which direction the element works.
Then we have the Location, which is just deﬁned as an integer. The Rail a
tuple of Locations. A SwitchRail the three Locations that is consists of and the
Direction of the switch-rail. The signal made up by the Location and Direction
types. And ﬁnally the RailwayNetwork as described in the table containing a
Location list, Rail list, SwitchRail list, Signal list and a list of the Trains and
their starting Location, goal Location and Direction.
Figure 3.3: The F# types used to describe the railway domain and game states.
Next up is the types needed for the states and game graph. These can also
be seen in Figure 3.3. Again they are mostly straight translations from Table 2.2
with a few adjustments. The SignalMap is a map with a Signal as the key and
boolean as the value. It is chosen to have a boolean as the value as a signal can
be either green or red, so we can easily represent green as being true and red as
being false. The TrainMap is the same as from the table. The key being a Train
(id) and the value its Location. As with the SignalMap the SwitchRailMap
also has a boolean as the value, but with a SwitchRail as the key. Again the
boolean is chosen as the switch-rail can be in one of two options. Either the
rail on the same row as the intersecting location is selected or the other one is.
True is here the same row rail being selected, and false as the other. Finally we
have the State type. We see that this can be one of two values either S of some
elements or N. Lets look at the S ﬁrst. The S constructor has ﬁve items, which
is more than was shown in the table from earlier. It now consists of an integer,
a SignalMap, a TrainMap, a SwitchRailMap and a State. The integer is needed
for being able to sort a list of states, used for heuristics, and will be used by the
solver. The three maps are the ones that tell the information about the state,
and match the information from the table. The State is used as a way to keep
track of the parent to the State, such that when a solution is found it is easy to
backtrack through the game graph. If looking at Figure 2.4 the State ﬁeld of a
state would be the state at the start of the arrow pointing at the state. The N
value exist as a null value for the State type, and is used as the parent of the
initial state, such that when backtracking we know when to stop.
19
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 4
Single Agent Solution
As mentioned the ﬁrst part of the problem was to create an eﬃcient control
program or solver. This will be done by looking at the problem as a single
agent problem, and not the multi agent problem it actually is. This is because
looking at it this way is a simpler problem, and ﬁnding a solution can be done
more eﬃciently. The solution itself might not be the most eﬃcient, but making
it more eﬃcient will be explored later. The basic idea for the single agent solver
is to use On-The-Fly generating of the states in the game graph. As explained
earlier this will be done by the two players of the game the Controller and
the Conductor. These can generate the states by changing signal values and
switch-rail values, or moving trains to new location if allowed. Then we will use
a Greedy Best-First [2] searching algorithm to pick the best of the generated
states, to try to ﬁnd a path to a goal state as fast and eﬃcient as possible.
The Best-First algorithm will use a simple goal heuristic, which means that the
shorter the total distance from the trains locations to their goal location is in a
state the better the state. As the goal of the problem is to get all the trains to
their goal location, using such a heuristic is a simple and good way to choose
between states. Before we start coding a few observations have been made, to
avoid generating states that are useless or irrelevant.
Lets look at the start of the game. As mentioned previously the setup of
the game only allows the Controller to act initially. However the Controller has
many options on what to do. The Controller is in charge of all the signals and
switch-rail, and is allowed to change any and as many as it wants to generate a
new state. This means that it can generate up to 2#Signal+#Switchrail new states
each turn. So in theory there are 2#Signal+#Switchrail edges from each of the
states in the game graph when it is the Controllers turn. This number grows
exceptionally fast and even for small railways it quickly becomes a problem.
However luckily for us most of these states are useless as they consists only of
changes to signals and switch-rails that in no way has any eﬀect on trains or
their ability to move. This means that we only need to consider signals and
switch-rails, which are directly connected to the locations of the trains. And
because we only look at a single agent problem at this time, there is no reason
20
==PAGE:22==
to look at more than a single train at a time. This means we can reduce the
number of generated state to O(#Train) for each Controller turn. So for each
state in which the Controller has its turn, we will only need to generate a small
number of new states, linear in the number of trains in the network. By doing
this has a great side eﬀect too. If we only make the Controller to change signals
and switch-rails which will allow a single train to move for each state, it means
that the Conductor only can generate one new state for each state generated
by the Controller. Because if only one train will be allowed to move at a time,
only one state can be generated. This results in the fact that the Conductor
therefore will never be able to branch in the game graph. Because to be able to
branch in the game graph it needs to be able to move multiple train. But since
it cannot, it means that any solution or control program found also will be a
correct. This means we wont have to backtrack through all the states generated
by the Conductor, to check if other options exists, because we know they do
not. So we just have to ﬁnd a single goal state to know that we have a correct
solution.
Greedy Best-First Solver
As mentioned before the way we are gonna ﬁnd a solution, is by using a greedy
best-ﬁrst searching algorithm to search through the generated states. The rea-
son we are going with a greedy best-ﬁrst and not a breadth-ﬁrst or depth-ﬁrst
[2] searching algorithm is because it often is the most eﬃcient way to ﬁnd a
path, and the other two each have their problems for this domain. We are not
necessarily looking for the shortest route as a breadth-ﬁrst would ﬁnd. Because
the branching value for each Controller node, is up to 2 times the number of
trains. This can quite quickly result in many states, and it not very eﬃcient.
The depth-ﬁrst algorithm also has its problems in this domain. Because not all
states lead to a goal state, it might end up picking a bad one at the beginning
and generating and exploring a lot of bad states, before ﬁnally coming back
to start and pick a better state. This again might be very ineﬃcient, and lots
of non-needed state could be generated. As mentioned we are also using state
generation on-the-ﬂy, meaning that the states are only generated when we need
them. This is necessary as creating the entire game state space, is just not
feasible. In most cases as there exists O(2|S|+|SR| ∗Q|L|
n=|L|−|T | n)1 states in the
games, which is quite impossible to keep track of and store in memory for even
smaller railways. So by only generating the states when we needed them, we
avoid having states that are not needed and will less likely run out of memory.
But before we can start implementing the state generation game and the
searching algorithm, we need to be able to load in the data from the railway
ﬁles. And we will need to process this data, such that we can easily generate
states and ﬁnd a solution.
1|X|, is the size of the list of X, where X is the symbols from Table 2.1.
21
==PAGE:23==
Loading Railway Networks
As mentioned in a previous section the railways are stored in .txt ﬁles, set up
in a speciﬁc way (Figure 3.1). This was done such that is would be easy to load
in the data to the program. The way this is done is by loading in all of the lines
from the ﬁle to a list. Such that the ﬁrst line in the ﬁle is the ﬁrst element in
the list. Then we go through the list one element at a time. Starting at the
ﬁrst element converting the string representation of the location to an integer
and adding to a list. This then continues picking the next element of the list
and doing the same until a colon is met. When this happens we know all the
locations have been loaded and the next elements are the rails. The rails are
loaded by splitting the line in two at the space, converting the two strings to
integers, and then creating a tuple of them. They are then added to a new list
which contains the rails. Again this continues until a colon is met, at which
point the rails will all have been loaded, and the switch-rails will begin. These
lines are also then split by the spaces, the three locations converted to integers
and the L or R string converted to the corresponding Direction type. The three
locations and the direction is then used to create a SwitchRail element and
added to a list. After the next colon the signals will be loaded, and then the
trains, in a similar manner. An in the end we will have a RailwayNetwork as
described in Table 2.1. The code used for this can be seen in the LoadFile.fs
ﬁle, and a snippet can be seen in Figure 4.1.
22
==PAGE:24==
Figure 4.1: Part of the code from the LoadFile.fs ﬁle. Used to load in railway
networks from a ﬁle.
Preprocessing
The next step before we can start searching for a solution is to process the data.
This is to create the initial state, and to create some data-structures that will
make searching through the game graph more eﬃcient.
We earlier described the State type, see Figure 3.3, which means that we
need to make the three maps it consists of to create the initial state. It was
also described how the maps works, so we just have to put the correct data in
23
==PAGE:25==
them. As mentioned in a previous section, the initial state is the state where all
signals are red, the switch-rails are set to one of the options and all the trains
are at their starting location. This means that to create the signal-map we need
to go through all the signals in the list from the loaded network. For each we
create a key using the signal and set the value to false, as this represents the
signal being red. For the train map we just have to go through the list of trains,
and create a key value pair for each, with the key being the train id, and the
value the starting location. For the switch-rail as mentioned, it does not matter
which of the two options is selected. So we just have to create a map, where the
switch-rail is the key and the value either true or false. For no particular reason
true is picked. This means the selected rail is the one that is in the same row
as the intersecting location of the switch-rail. The ﬁnal two items needed to
create the initial state element is an integer and another element of type State.
The integer is used for the heuristic and is not relevant for the initial state, as
it will be picked ﬁrst since it is the only state, and is therefore just set to 0.
The State type element is set to N. This was as mentioned the null value of the
State type, and as the initial state does not have a parent node setting this to
null or N makes sense. The code snippet for creating the initial state can be
seen in Figure 4.2.
Figure 4.2: Code snippet from the Preprocessing.fs ﬁle part of the InitiateState
function. Used to create the initial state.
Next up some useful data-structures are created. This includes a list of tuple
containing train ids and the direction of the train, a map to get the goal location
given a train id, a map to get a switch-rail given a rail, two RailwayGraphs and
a DistanceMap. The three ﬁrst are used to quickly look up information when
searching the game graph, whereas the last two are more interesting. The two
RailwayGraphs are graphs over the railway network, where the locations are
the nodes and the rails and switch-rails are the edges. These will be used to
ﬁnd the paths the trains can use to get from their starting location to their goal
location. The reason there are two graphs is because they are directed, since the
trains are only allowed to move in one direction. So one is for moving left and
the other is for moving right. The way they are implemented are as adjacency
lists in a map. Such that each location of the network is a key in the map, and
all the locations that it can reach by a single rail, in the direction of the map,
are put in a list as the value. The way these are made is by ﬁrst going through
all the locations adding them as keys to a map, with an empty list as values.
Then the rails are gone through one by one, where the largest number of the
two locations, the rail consists of, is picked as key and the second is added to
the value list of that key. This way we are ensuring that all of these are in the
24
==PAGE:26==
same direction for going left, as we know all rails are only connecting locations
on the same row, due to how the editor works. With the larger number of two
locations being the one further to the right. Then all the switch-rails are looked
through. Here we look at the direction of the switch-rail, and add the correct
locations to the correct lists, such that their are for going left in the railway.
We now have one of the RailwayGraphs, and can then create the other by going
through this one, and reverse what is contains. The code to do this can be seen
in Figure 4.3.
Figure 4.3: Code snippet from the Preprocessing.fs ﬁle from the InitiateState
function. Used to create the RailwayGraphs.
The second interesting data-structure we want to create is the DistanceMap.
As mentioned the greedy best-ﬁrst algorithm will use a goal heuristic as a mea-
surement to pick the next state to explore. So each state has to know its heuristic
value. This is done by summing up the total distance for each train from their
current position in the state to their goal location. In order to not calculate
this distance at each state generation, a distance-map is created. This contains
all distances from any location to another as long as a paths exists between the
two. That way instead of having to calculate it each time we can just look it
up, to speed up the process. The way this distance-map is created is by picking
a location, look up in the one of railway-graph what locations it can reach and
then add the tuple of the two locations as the key, with 1 as the value. Then for
each of the reachable locations look through the same railway-graph for what
they can reach, add them to the map by creating a tuple of the original location
and the new location with the value of 2. The is repeated until no more new lo-
cations are found in the railway-graph. This is then repeated for every location
to get the full distance-map. The function to do this can be seen in Figure 4.4.
Figure 4.4: Code snippet from the Preprocessing.fs ﬁle the CreateDistanceMap
function. Used to create the distance-map.
The ﬁnal thing that is done in the preprocessing is ﬁnding all locations,
which are on a path from a starting location of a train to the goal location. As
the trains are not allowed to move in reverse, it means that there are certain
locations of the railway which are unreachable. And more important there are
locations from which the goal locations are unreachable. So to guide the trains
25
==PAGE:27==
to avoid the locations from which their goal is unreachable, which would result
in states from which a goal state can not be found, all the locations which the
trains can reach, and the goal location is reachable are calculated. This is done,
by ﬁnding all the locations that are reachable going in the train’s direction from
the starting location, and intersecting it with all the locations that are reachable
going the the opposite direction from the goal location. This is the exact set of
locations, which are reachable for the train, and from which the goal location
can be reached. These locations set are found by going through the railway-
graph starting at a location and following nodes and edges until no more are
found. The code to do this can be seen in Figure 4.5.
Figure 4.5: Code snippet from the Preprocessing.fs ﬁle the FindPaths function.
Used to ﬁnd all location that each train can reach, from which their goal location
is also reachable.
We have now done all the preprocessing we need and can ﬁnally start creating
the game and solver.
The Game
So the way were are gonna ﬁnd a solution is by looking at the problem as a game
with two players. We have the Controller who can change values of signals and
switch-rails, and the Conductor who can move the trains. The way a solution is
found, is if the Controller wins the game, which is done by ﬁnding a goal state.
We are gonna implement the game as two diﬀerent functions ControllerTurn
and ConductorTurn. As mentioned numerous times, the Conductor is not able
to do anything in the initial state, so lets look at the ControllerTurn function
ﬁrst.
As a way to keep track of the best states, we will added all states generated
to a priority-queue, which will keep track of the state sorted best to worst,
based on the heuristic described earlier. The way the Controller then starts is
by popping the queue to get the best state. It will then check whether or not
this is a goal state. If it is we are done, we have found a solution. Otherwise
26
==PAGE:28==
the Controller gets its turn. First it will create a number of new states, one
for each train. Each of these states will have a single the change, which is that
the signal at the location of the train is turned green. All other signals will be
red. All the generated states are then added to the priority-queue and to a set
of states, if they pass two checks. The ﬁrst check it has to pass is done by the
function IsSafeState, the code can be seen in Figure 4.6. This function checks
it the state is safe. A safe state is a state which has three properties.
Figure 4.6: Code snippet from the BFSSolver.fs ﬁle the IsSafeState function.
Used to check if a state does not break the three properties for being a safe
state.
The ﬁrst is that no train must be able to reach the location of another train.
Being able to reach, means that the train will have a open path of available rails
and switch-rails with no red signals to the other location. If this is not kept
it means that when the Conductor gets their turn, they will be able to move
a train to another trains location, and thereby making the Controller lose the
game. So to avoid this, no train must be able to reach another trains location,
in a state generated by the Controller.
The second property is that no train, must be able to reach a location that
is also reachable by another train. The reason for this is the same as for the
ﬁrst property. If two trains can reach the same location the Conductor will be
able to crash the trains and win the game. So the Controller should also not
add states for which this property does not hold.
The ﬁnal property is that no train should be able to reach a location from
which the goal location is not reachable. If this is not the case the Conductor,
will be able to move the train to a location from which the Controller will no
longer be able to guide it to its goal. Thereby the Controller will not be able
to win the game, as a goal state will not be anywhere on this path in the game
graph.
If it passes this ﬁrst check, we know that this state will not make the Con-
troller lose, when giving the turn to the Conductor, and therefore is safe to keep.
Because if the three properties hold when the Conductor gets the state, they
27
==PAGE:29==
will also hold in the state generated by the Conductor performing an action, if
the rules of the game are followed. This is because if no signal or switch-rail
changes are made, which can only be made by the Controller, moving a train to
another legal location, will only make the number of reachable locations for the
train smaller. You remove the location it was just at from the set of reachable
locations. Because if a location was not reachable before moving the train, it
will not be reachable after moving the train, as no signals or switch-rail selec-
tions can have changed, which is the only way to add new locations to being
reachable. This means that if all states generated by the Controller have these
properties, the Conductor will never be able to make the Controller lose, and
thereby when we do ﬁnd a solution we know all the states are safe and the
solution therefore is correct.
The second check a state has to pass, before being added to the priority-
queue and state set, is a duplication check. This is done to avoid adding states
that either already are in the queue or has been in the queue. The way this
is done, is that we are keeping track of all states that have been added to the
queue at some point, by storing a hash-value for each state. Then when a new
state is to be added, we hash it and check if the hash value already exists. If
not it passes the check and can be added, otherwise we just throw it away. We
are using hash-values instead of storing all the states to reduce the amount of
memory needed and speed up the process. Because storing a single number
uses less space, and comparing two numbers takes less time than comparing
two states. There might however occur the problem, if the hashing function is
not good enough, that a state that has not been added has a the same hash-
value as another state already added. In this case we throw away a state, which
should not have been thrown away. This was actually a problem early in the
development, because the solver threw away states that were needed for ﬁnding
a solution due to hashing collisions. So instead of just using the built in hash-
function in F# it was modiﬁed it a bit, after which the problem not longer
occurred. The modiﬁed hash-function can be seen in Figure 4.7.
Figure 4.7: Code snippet from the BFSSolver.fs ﬁle the hash function. Used to
hash a state.
The states that pass the checks are then added to a state set and to the
priority-queue.
Next we ﬁnd all the switch-rails if any, which share a location with a train.
For each of these we will create a new state, where the switch-rail has been
switched, and the signal turned green. Again all other signals are changed to
red. And as before for these states to get added to the set and priority-queue,
they have to pass the same checks as before. The Controller will now have
generated all the relevant states for all trains, and the next step is to hand
over the states generated from the set and give the turn to the Conductor. The
28
==PAGE:30==
ControllerTurn code, can be seen in Figure 4.8.
Figure 4.8: Code snippet from the BFSSolver.fs ﬁle part of the ControllerTurn
function. Used to generate states as the Controller.
When the Conductor gets its turn, the ﬁrst thing it does is to check if
any states were provided by the Controller. If not the turn is return to the
Controller. If it has been given states, then for each state it will generate one
new state. In this state all movable trains, which is only a single one, due to how
the Controller generates states, have been moved one location forward. These
new states will then, if passing the duplication check, be added to the priority-
queue and a new set of states. There is no need to see, if they pass the safe
state check, because as mentioned the Conductor cannot break the properties,
if they hold in the states it is provided. Then the Conductor will get its turn
again. This is to simulate the situation, where the Controller decides not to do
anything with its turn, as this is not covered by the ControllerTurn function.
This will in fact often be the best choice for the Controller, as the trains spent a
lot of time on rail sections, where the it has no ability to manipulate where they
are moving. The code the generate the states, and the ConductorTurn function
can be seen in Figure 4.9.
29
==PAGE:31==
Figure 4.9: Code snippets from the BFSSolver.fs ﬁle the ConductorTurn func-
tion and GenerateConductorStates function.
Used to generate states as the
Conductor.
When no more states are generated the turn is returned to the Controller,
and the game will go on until either a goal state is found, which means we have
a solution, or we run out of states to explore. When a solution is found, we
create a list of all the states in the solution, by going through all the parents of
the states, starting with the goal state. This list of states can then be seen as
the control program.
This solver, and all the modiﬁed versions have all been tested on 7 railway
networks, the results can be seen in the Results section of the report. This solver
is the Single Agent Version 1 (SAv1). Other networks have also been used to
test the solvers, but they have not been included in this report.
Observations
When testing the solver on the railway networks, a few things was noticed. First
is that if a network has too many trains, the solver could not ﬁnd a solution
in reasonable time. This is likely due to the branching factor of the Controller,
which generates up to two times the number of trains new states each turn,
which quickly can become a problem. Another problem the solver has, is when
the number of paths to a goal state is low. Or said in another way, when the
solution has a very speciﬁc order the trains should be moved. This is due to
the heuristic of the algorithm, it could easily chose a path of states moving a
train very close to its goal. But if another train is blocking the goal, it might
have to backtrack a lot before ﬁnding a way to get the other train away ﬁrst.
Because it would at this point prioritize picking states, where other trains are
moved, but this does not solve the issue of the blocking train. Which means all
of these states are generated and looked at in vein, as they will never lead to a
goal state. A last thing that was noticed was that for the networks the solution
often consisted of moving one train in goal at a time. This a good way to solve
the problem. So if the solution often ends up moving a single train to goal at a
30
==PAGE:32==
time, there is no need to generate |T| states each time the Controller takes its
turn. If it anyway is gonna pick the states in some order of moving the trains
one by one, maybe it would be possible to learn this order before hand, and
then only generate one state each time the Controller acts.
Priorities and Swapping
So the two biggest problem with the current solver is that it cannot solve large
networks in a fair amount of time, and if the way the trains have to move is too
speciﬁc. It was also noticed that the trains often were moved to goal one by
one, which is a good idea for solving the networks. So lets look a bit more into
this, to see if we can somehow help the solver by ﬁnding the order of the trains
beforehand, instead of during the searching. The idea is to give each train a
priority, such that the higher the priority the sooner the train should be moved
to its goal location. So lets look at how we can do this. A train initially can
be in one of two cases. Either it has a path from the starting location to the
goal that is free, meaning no other train is placed on any of the locations of the
path, or it does not. It is easy to see that if nothing is blocking the train from
reaching its goal, we could just move it to its goal, and thereby we will have
one less train to worry about. However before doing so, we need to make sure
that this train will not, if placed in goal, be blocking other trains from reaching
their goal. Otherwise placing it in goal, will not help us ﬁnd a solution, only
make it more diﬃcult or even impossible. The second case of trains is where
something is blocking them from reaching their goal, either by a train being on
their goal location or blocking all possible paths from the start location to the
goal location. It is clear here that we need to move the other train away ﬁrst,
otherwise this train cannot reach its goal. So the way we are going to prioritize
the trains, is by giving the highest priorities to the trains with free paths to
their goal. And if something is blocking a train, the blocking train will need
at higher priority than it, in order to get it to move away ﬁrst. In general this
will work for many networks, as moving free trains to goals often will open up
paths for other trains, that then freely can move to goal. However there is one
big problem, what if all the trains are blocked by other trains? Lets say train
T1 needs to go to the location of T2, but T3 is placed in the goal of T2, and
T3 cannot get to goal either because T1 is there. How should these trains be
prioritized? The solution to this is actually rather simple. Get one of the trains
to move to a location from which the others will not be blocked. Now one of
the trains will have a free path to goal, and when moved the next train has an
open path. And when the last train in this cycle has been moved to its goal,
the ﬁrst train that was moved aside can be moved to its goal. So if we always
can move trains aside, we should always be able to ﬁnd a way to prioritize the
trains. Done by picking them in order of having a free path to goal. So this is
the idea lets look at how it can be implemented.
31
==PAGE:33==
Preprocessing
As the idea was to prioritize the trains before the game graph generation and
searching, most of the work is gonna be done in preprocessing. The ﬁrst thing
that needs to be done is that instead of just having a set of all locations between
the starting location of a train and its goal, we need to ﬁnd all the distinct paths
between the two locations. This is such that we can see if a train has a free
path, or if all of them are blocked by other trains.
To do this we add the starting location of a train to a list. The we look in
the railway-graph for the direction of the train, what locations the location at
the head of the list can reach. If it can reach a single location we add this as
the new head of the list, and then do the it again. If it can reach two locations,
because of a switch-rail, will we duplicate the list, such that we have two. Where
the ﬁrst element of one of the lists is the one new location and in the other the
other new location. We then continue, now doing it for both lists. When we
reach a location which cannot reach other locations, this path is done. When
all paths are done, we have a set of location lists, with all paths leaving the
starting location of a train direction, with the elements in reverse order. As not
all of these necessarily lead to the goal location of the train, and some of them
might pass it, we need to clean them up. This is done by going through each list
one by one, and removing elements until the goal location is found. When it is
found we know that the list only contains the locations in between the start and
goal locations of the train, so we keep this list. If the goal location is not found,
we will at some point run out of elements in the list, and the list is removed.
When done with all the lists, we now have all the distinct paths between the
start and goal location of a train. This is then done for all the trains in the
network. The code can be seen in Figure 4.10.
Figure 4.10: Code snippet from the Preprocess.fs ﬁle the DistinctPaths function.
Used to ﬁnd all distinct paths between the start and goal locations of the trains.
Now that we have found all distinct paths for all trains, we can check if any
32
==PAGE:34==
of the trains, are part of these cycles of trains blocking each other. These will
be known as swap-cycles. There are two ways that a train can be part of a
swap-cycle. Either directly because it is placed on the goal location of another
train, and its own goal location has another train placed on it. Or indirectly
by blocking all paths of another train with its goal and starting locations. So
we look at these cases separately and then combine what is found. The directly
blocking trains are easy to ﬁnd, we just go through the trains and check if their
goal is the starting location of another train, and if their starting location is the
goal of another train. If so check the other trains involved, if it is also the case
for them, continue until it either is not the case or one of the trains is found
again. If found again we have a swap-cycle, if not found the cycle is broken
at some point, meaning one of the trains should not have its goal immediately
blocked, and therefore have a free path to goal. Which when moved opens up
for the next train and so on. The code to do this can be seen in Figure 4.11.
Figure 4.11: Code snippet from the Preprocess.fs ﬁle the SwapCycle function.
Used to ﬁnd all directly connected swap-cycles.
The indirect swap-cycles are a bit more complicated to ﬁnd. To do this for
each train we need to check if all of its possible paths are being blocked by a
train. Meaning that the start and goal location of a train both has to be on a
path of another train to block it. If so that path is blocked. So we go through
all of the distinct paths a train has and check if all of them are blocked. If there
is one that is not, the train has a free path, and nothing will be done. If all
the paths are blocked, we ﬁnd which trains are involved, and add them all to
a set. This does not necessarily ﬁnd the entire swap-cycle, that these train are
part of, but we ﬁx that part later. But having a train block all the paths of
another means that those two as a minimum have a swap-cycle going on, and
one needs to be moved aside for the other to get to goal. The code for ﬁnding
these swap-cycles can be seen in Figure 4.12.
33
==PAGE:35==
Figure 4.12: Code snippet from the Preprocess.fs ﬁle the SpecialSwapCycle
function. Used to ﬁnd all train indirectly connected in swap-cycles.
We now might have a lot of small sets of trains in swap-cycles, that are ac-
tually connected. So the next step is to combine all of the set, which have trains
in common. This is simply done by taking two sets and checking if they have
any train in common. If so combine them, if not keep both. Then take another
set, and check against the either combined set or the two non-overlapping sets,
and combine them if any trains are overlapping. This is continued until all the
sets have been checked, and combined if needed. The resulting sets, are the the
swap-cycles of the network, Figure 4.13.
Figure 4.13: Code snippet from the Preprocess.fs ﬁle the FindSwapCycle func-
tion. Used to ﬁnd swap-cycles in a network.
Now that we have found all the swap-cycles, if any in the network, we can
move on to the next step. Here we will go through all of the swap-cycles just
found, and for each ﬁnd a train which can be move aside, while the others are
then moved to their goal. This is done by going through all the trains in a swap-
cycle, and ﬁnding all of the locations which the train can safely move to without
blocking other trains, and still reach its goal location. Each location is then
gone through, checking that the train can be blocked here by the Controller.
Meaning that there has to be a signal. This is important as the Conductor
otherwise might move the train to another location, which will not be safe.
Having a switch-rail, is also a way the Controller can block the train, but since
all switch-rails needs signals, we will ﬁnd all locations just by looking for signals.
When this is done the train, which can be moved the furthest to get to a safe
location, is picked as the train that should be moved aside for this swap-cycle.
A new goal-map is then created, setting this safe location as the goal for the
picked train, such that when we get to the game, to Controller will at ﬁrst
believe this is where the train should go. The same is done for the paths of the
train, such that it will not be guided the wrong way later. This is done for all
34
==PAGE:36==
the swap-cycles, such that we will now have two goal-maps, the ﬁrst guiding the
picked trains to their safe locations, and the other trains to their goals, and the
same is done for path-maps. Next up we need to update the paths for the rest
of the trains, such that they do not believe that they can use the paths that
will be blocked by the safe trains. Then ﬁnally we will check if in these new
conditions there still exists swap-cycles, and if so, do the same thing over again
until it is not the case. The implementation can be seen in Figure 4.14.
Figure 4.14: Code snippet from the Preprocess.fs ﬁle the SplitWork function.
Used to ﬁnd safe locations for train in swap-cycles, to move trains aside such
that the swap-cycles are dissolved.
By doing it this way, creating two goal-maps, we have split up the solving
into two rounds.
The ﬁrst pass is when the trains are moved to their safe
locations, and the rest to their goal. The second pass is then when the trains at
their safe location have to be moved to their actual goal locations. This means
that we need to be very careful not to place any trains in the ﬁrst pass in such a
way that they will block trains in the second pass. So to avoid this the next step
is to ﬁnd the distinct paths that the trains in the second pass can take. This is
done using a setup where the safe train have their starting location as being the
safe location, and the goal their original goal. We can then combine these with
the paths that were found for the trains to move to their safe location from the
start, to get all the distinct paths that these trains actually have. Now that we
force them to go to a certain location in between. That way we will know later
if we decides to move a non-safe train, if it will block one of the safe trains. Now
that we ﬁnally have found a way to avoid having swap-cycles in the network,
we can start ﬁguring out how to prioritize the trains.
The way this is done is by ﬁrst temporarily remove all paths that are not
usable in the initial setup, with all the trains at their starting location. This
is the paths where at least one of the locations in the path is the starting
35
==PAGE:37==
location of another train. When done, we know that all of the paths that are
left are entirely free. We then go through each train, and check if they have any
remaining paths. If not this train cannot currently be moved. If it does we then
check if placing the train in its current goal location, either the actual goal, or
if it was selected as a safe train before the safe location for the ﬁrst pass, will
result in blocking any other trains from being able to reach their goal. If it does
block the train should not be moved yet and we move on, otherwise we add it
to a set. When done looking through all the trains we will have a set of trains,
which in the current setup can be move to goal because it has a free path and
does not block other trains if placed in goal. For the code see Figure 4.15.
Figure 4.15: Code snippet from the Preprocess.fs ﬁle from the CalculatePrior-
ities function. Used to ﬁnd trains that have a free path, and does not block
other trains if placed at goal location.
The next step is to ﬁrst check if any trains were actually added to the set.
If not the function returns the current priority map, which is empty the ﬁrst
round, and the set of paths that the trains can take. If we did ﬁnd some trains,
we will look at these trains, to see if it is possible for some of them to move at
the same time. This is done by picking a train from the set and its shortest free
path. The shortest path is the path which contains the fewest vertical moves.
As the railways all are based on a grid with each rail having the same length,
all the paths contains the same amount of locations. So to model that using a
switch-rail to change lane is actually longer, the shortest path is the one with
the fewest of these. When the path has been selected the other trains from the
set are looked at to see if any of these have a free path, which has no overlap in
locations with the just selected path. Because if a train does, it means that the
two trains are completely independent of each other, and will be able to move
at the same time. After this the set with the most trains which has non-crossing
paths is selected, see Figure 4.16.
36
==PAGE:38==
Figure 4.16: Code snippet from the Preprocess.fs ﬁle from the CalculatePriori-
ties function. Used to ﬁnd trains that have free non-crossing paths.
The path-map for all the selected trains is then updated, to only contain one
speciﬁc path for each train which is the non-crossing just found. The paths for
all other trains are updated to no longer containing paths which requires the
goal location of the selected trains, as these no longer are usable. The locations
of the trains are updated to now represent the setup where the selected trains
are in goal, and the rest at their start location. And an equal priority value is
given to all the selected trains. The function is then called recursively on this
new setup, and will in the next round give the trains that are selected a priority
of one less than just given. At some point the function does not ﬁnd any more
free trains, either because all have been placed in goal and gotten a priority. Or
because some of the train never had a free path or would always be blocking if
placed in goal. If the ﬁrst is the case nothing happens in the next step. If not
these trains are then added as belonging to the second pass, and their goal in
the ﬁrst pass is updated to being the same as their starting location to avoid the
Controller trying to moved them in the game later before they should. All the
train that received a priority also received an exact path for it to take, which is
also stored. We then create the setup for the second pass, where all trains that
did get a priority are placed in their goal location or their safe location, and the
goal is then set as the actual goal location for all the trains. We then ﬁnd the
distinct paths for the trains that are yet to be in goal, and then call the priority
function again on this setup to give priorities to the remaining trains. When
done hopefully all trains have gotten a priority, if it happens that some have
not, they will be given a priority of zero. The preprocessing is now done, and
we now have a lot more data-structures that should be sent on to the Controller
than before. Which includes two goal-maps, one for the ﬁrst pass and one for
the second. Two path-maps, again one for each pass, and the priority-map with
the priorities of the trains.
The Game
Now that we have a bit more new data from the preprocessing, we need to make
a few changes to the solver. The ﬁrst is that when checking if the networks has
37
==PAGE:39==
been solved, we need to ﬁrst use the ﬁrst pass goal-map, and when this would
return true, update the goal-map and path-map to the second pass versions.
Because when returning true, it means all the trains are at their ﬁrst pass goals,
and we then have to move on to the second pass of the game. This is fairly
simple, but needs to be done, otherwise the solver will think it is done, before
it is. Also when the ﬁrst pass has been cleared, we can remove all states still
in the priority-queue, as the second pass uses the goal state of the ﬁrst pass as
the initial state. If not done we might end up picking a ﬁrst pass state, but
try to move the train according to their second pass goals and paths, which
will give problems. This is simply done by modifying the IsSolved function, see
Figure 4.17.
Figure 4.17: Code snippet from the BFSSolver.fs ﬁle the updated IsSolved func-
tion. Used to check if the game is solved, and now update goal- and path-maps,
when ﬁrst pass is over.
The next change is in the ControllerTurn function. Instead of creating a new
state for each train, we will now ﬁnd the trains with the highest priority, that
are not in goal. We will then pick one of these and only create one new state.
The one where we turn the signal the train is waiting behind green, and possibly
change the switch-rail such that the correct rail is selected according to the path
it needs to take. When the turn is then returned to the Controller, it will pick
this train again if not in goal. When in goal another train will be picked, the
same way as the ﬁrst was selected, as the one with the highest priority not yet
in goal. This will continue until the game is solved, such that the train with the
highest priority is moved to goal ﬁrst and the one with the lowest last. For the
code see Figure 4.18.
38
==PAGE:40==
Figure 4.18: Code snippet from the BFSSolver.fs ﬁle from the updated Con-
trollerTurn function. Used to pick train with highest priority not in goal, and
generate state for it to move forward.
If it so happens, that some of the trains was given the priority of zero in the
preprocessing, the Controller will use the old way to try and get these to their
goal location. This will happen after the trains that do have a priority have
been move in goal. We only look at these after the prioritized trains because
we know that if a train is given a priority it means that it will not block other
trains from being able to reach goal. And therefore if a solution does exist, we
should be able to ﬁnd it when they are in goal. This version of the solver is the
Single Agent version 2 (SAv2).
Another Game
We know that if a train has a priority, it means that it has a free path to goal,
when it is the highest priority train not yet in goal. Because it was given the
priority, when the trains with higher priority was in goal, and lower priority at
their starting locations. And it would only have been picked and given a priority
if it had a free path. We can use this knowledge to reduce the number of times
the Controller has to act. Because what if we just open up the entire path
for the train when it has been selected as having the highest priority. Meaning
that all the signals on the path will be turned green and the switch-rail changed
to match the path needed to be taken by the train. That way the Controller
would only have to act once per train, given they all have a priority, which
would reduce the number of state the Controller has to generate.
39
==PAGE:41==
This is actually very easy to do as well, because when we did give priorities
to the trains, we also picked out the exact path it should take. So when we
have selected a train, we can just go through each location in the path, check
if a signal is present in the direction of the train. If so turn it green. Then
go through all the switch-rails on the path and change them such that they
guide the train on the correct path. That way the Controller will only have
to generate this single state for each train. The code for this can be seen in
Figure 4.19. This is the Single Agent version 3 (SAv3) solver.
Figure 4.19: Code snippet from the BFSSolver.fs ﬁle, the OpenPath function
and from another updated ControllerTurn function. Used to open an entire path
for a train, such that all signals are green, and switch-rails changed correctly.
Used to make the Controller only generate one state per train.
These three single agent solvers will always ﬁnd a correct control program.
This is as mentioned due to the fact that only one train is allowed to move
in each state generated by the Controller. Meaning that the Conductor cannot
branch in the game graph, which means that a solution is also a correct solution,
as the Conductor never has an option to act diﬀerently.
40
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 5
Multi Agent Solution
We now have a seemingly very eﬃcient single agent solver, which does not seem
to generate unused states for most networks. However none of the solutions
are very eﬃcient, understood as the length of the solutions are close to worst
case. This is of cause due to the fact that they only ever allow a single train
to move at a time, so to get a more eﬃcient solution, we will need to look at
the problem as a multi agent problem. We have multiple options of how we
want to implement the multi agent solver. One way is to use the knowledge
gained when creating the single agent solver, and try to make a multi agent
solver, that works in a similar way. But instead of only allowing a single train
to move at a time, allow all or some of them, based on the priority of the trains
or other factors. Another way to do it is to use the solution found by the single
agent solver and merge the states, such that in the end it will look like a multi
agent solution, even though it was created with being a single agent solution
in mind. A similar idea is explored by Luis, Fern´andez and Borrajo [4]. Both
ideas have their pros and cons. One of the pros of the ﬁrst idea, and con of the
second, is that the solution does not depend on how it was possible to move the
trains one by one, which might allow for some smarter movement, that is not
possible when solving single agently. A con is that we might start to generate
a lot of states again, and might end up in some of the situations of the ﬁrst
single agent solver, where the branching factor becomes to big. Theoretically
we might end having the Controller generate up to 2|T| states each time, if
we want to check all possibilities, and ﬁnd the best solution. We could also
