only allow a small number of trains to move at a time. This would reduce the
branching factor, but might end up giving some weird solutions, where trains
are not moving even though it technically should not be a problem if they did,
due to the selection of trains. It also has the same problem as the ﬁrst single
agent solver with networks where only one or few paths exists that leads to a
solution, moving a train to far too soon will lead to problems. The second idea
does not have these problems. Because here we already have a solution. We just
have to look through it and see if we can merge the states, such that the three
proprieties are not broken, and the edges in the game graph are still legal. One
41
==PAGE:43==
might think that a problem could be that some networks require a multi agent
solution to be solved, which would be a problem for the second idea, as it builds
on a single agent solution, and therefore these networks would not be solvable.
This is not true however. It cannot be the case that it only has a multi agent
solution. Because lets say such a correct solution does exists. This means that
one edge in the game graph requires that multiple trains are moving. But for
it to be a correct solution all edges from this state has to lead to a goal state,
also the one where just a single train moves, cause the Conductor might select
only to move a single train. So if it indeed is a correct solution following this
edge should also lead to a goal state. And the same for all other edges in the
graph. In each case the Conductor has the option to only move one train, so if a
correct multi agent solution exists, a single agent solution also exists. Another
con of the second approach is that the multi agent solution is based on a single
agent solution, which might lead to a less optimal solution, as it might have
been possible to ﬁnd a smarter path if searching for a multi agent solution from
the beginning. Another con for the ﬁrst idea is that when a solution is found
we need to back track through all the states. Because we need to check all the
places the Conductor could have done something diﬀerent, to make sure these
edges also lead to a goal state, to ensure we have a correct solution. This is
not a problem for the other idea, as we base it on a correct solution. So if the
combining of states is done correctly it should not break the correctness, as the
other paths or options the Conductor could have taken, should all in the end
be boiled down to the single agent edges, which we knows comes from a correct
solution.
The idea we are going to go with is the second. This is due to the fact that
we already have a very eﬃcient single agent solver, and that we are sure when
merging, that if done correctly, the solution will still be correct. We now also
move a bit away from the idea that the Conductor would try to sabotage the
Controller. This does not mean that the solution we try to ﬁnd will not be
correct, but we want to ﬁnd the fastest solution, which requires the two players
to play together. This also means that we wont have an exact control plan
for the solution as with the single agent version. Where it was possible to tell
exactly when the signals and switch-rails should be changed, but now will have
more of a guideline as to when this can be done. This will be explain more when
we get to it.
Multi Agent Version 1
The ﬁrst version of a multi agent solution is just a small extension of the third
single agent solver. Instead of just picking one of the trains with the highest
priority of trains not in goal, the Controller picks all of them. Then it opens
the paths for all picked trains. This works because when the trains were given a
priority, they were only given the same, if they had non-crossing free paths. This
means that opening the paths for multiple trains of the same priority, will not
violate any of the three properties as the paths are non overlapping. Therefore
42
==PAGE:44==
the trains, will not be able to reach each other or the same locations. And from
all the locations they can reach the goal location is also reachable, because the
paths that have been opened is the paths from the starting locations to the
goals of the trains. Implementing this is super simple, as the preprocessing has
already been done earlier, all that needs to be done is remove the ﬁrst line from
second code snippet in Figure 4.19, where it picks the minimum element. That
way all the trains with the same priority will be picked and have their paths
opened up. This can also be done for the second single agent solver, by doing
something similar. However this leads to solutions where trains that will wait
at signals until all other trains of the same priority is also stopped by a signal.
Which looks wrong, and weird. This is also basically just a slower version than
opening the entire path, so it has not been tested as the others.
But is the solution it ﬁnds also a correct solution? It can easily be seen that
this is the case, without having to backtrack through all states generated by
the Conductor, to check all edges. The reason is that the Controller will not do
anything new until all the trains of a priority have been placed in goal. So even
if the Conductor decides to move the trains one by one, or ﬁrst one then another
and then a third, or in other non optimal ways. All of these will always end up
leading to the same state, where all the trains of this priority is in goal. Because
they have nowhere else to go, and can only follow the path which leads them
to goal. Also none of these states, will have violated the properties. Because
as mention earlier the Conductor cannot break a property if it holds in the
last state generated by the Controller which it does. So even if the Conductor
should move the trains in a none linear way, it will always end up with the trains
in goal, and a correct solution. This will be known as the Multi Agent version
1 (MAv1). The biggest problem with this solution as a multi agent solution is
that it requires that trains have independent paths. If for example a network
only has a single rail in the middle connecting two sides, and all of the trains
have to cross, this solution would just be a single agent solution. Even though
it quite clearly could be the case that the train can move at the same time, just
not cross that one rail.
Multi Agent Version 2
Now we are going to start looking at the method described in the start of the
chapter, with using the merging single agent solutions to create a multi agent
solution. The basis for this one is the MAv1 solution. This is of cause not
a single agent solution, but using this instead of SAv3 will save a few steps.
Because the MAv1 solution is basically a merged SAv3, but only for the train
of the same priority. So there is no reason not to use this, as it will just make it
slower not to. The idea we are going to look at is that instead of waiting until
trains are in goal to open the paths for the next priority trains, we will ﬁnd the
earliest state where the path is free and then open it up. That way the trains
will in a lot of cases start moving earlier, and thereby a faster solution can be
found. The way we are going to do this is as mentioned by looking at the MAv1
43
==PAGE:45==
solution for a network, and then merge states to get a hopefully faster solution.
The ﬁrst thing that will be done is to turn the signals red when a train has
passed them. This is done simply by checking the ﬁrst state and the second
state against each other, to see if any trains have changed location. If so the
locations of the trains in the ﬁrst state are saved, and the signal map for the
second state is updated such that if a signal is present it is changed to red at
these locations. The second state is then checked against the third state the
same way. If a train is moving again the location from the second state is also
stored, and the signal map for the third state is updated to make signals from
all the stored locations red. This then goes on until there is no train movement
between states. Because at this point we know that the trains with the highest
priority have reach their goals, and the Controller has opened up the paths for
the next set of trains. Here all the saved location are trashed, as they no longer
are needed because the Controller will have turned oﬀall signals before turning
on the ones on the paths for the next trains. The same thing is then repeated
until we run out of states. The code can be seen in Figure 5.1.
Figure 5.1: Code snippet from the PostProcess.fs ﬁle the TurnOﬀSignals func-
tion. Used to turn signals to red when a train has passed them.
This technically results in illegal edges in the game graph, as we both turn
signals red and move trains at the same time. However it is done as a measure
to reduce the length of the solution, as turning signals red, behind trains, in
no way can make the solution un-correct, as it has no impact on the reachable
locations of a train. Also if seen in a real life scenario it makes sense to turn
signals red the second the train has passed.
The next step is to split the solution states into smaller lists only containing
trains of a certain priority moving. This is such that we know when each priority
of trains are starting to move, and that way know which states that makes sense
looking into merging together. To do this we used the original solution state list,
not the one which had the signals turned red, to check when the signal-map is
changing between states. Because if the signal-map has changed between states,
it means that the next priority of trains will start to move, because this is a
Controller generated state. The code snippet for this can be seen in Figure 5.2.
We can do this because the state list with the turned oﬀsignals, will contains
the same states in the same order, just where the signals are red, meaning that
44
==PAGE:46==
when we ﬁnd the split in the original state list it will be at the same spot in the
new one.
Figure 5.2: Code snippet from the PostProcess.fs ﬁle the SplitStateList function.
Used to split the state list into smaller lists, each containing a set of states where
only a single priority of trains are moving.
Next up is the merging part. This is done by picking the ﬁrst two state
lists just found. These are the ones containing the states where the highest and
second highest priority trains are moving. The ﬁrst list is the base list, and the
second is the one we want to merge into the ﬁrst. But ﬁrst we need to ﬁnd out
which locations that the trains in the second list are using. Because these are
the ones that needs to be free in the states that we want to merge into. This is
simply done by going through the states in the list and checking the train-map
for which trains are moving, and saving all the locations involved. Then we ﬁnd
which trains are moving in the ﬁrst state list. This is needed to ﬁnd the point
in the list where the merging can start. Which lead to the next step, which is
ﬁnding the exact state in the ﬁrst list, where the locations used in the second
list are free, and no longer will be used. This is done by going through the states
one by one and checking if a train is using one of the locations or a signal which
is green is on one of the locations. If this is the case the next state is checked
and so on. If not we found the state where the merging can begin. All the
states found which could not be merged are stored as the ﬁrst states in the ﬁnal
multi agent solution. To merge the states we pick the ﬁrst state that we found
as mergeable from the ﬁrst list, and the ﬁrst state of the second list that we
wanted to merge into it. We then create a new state, where the signal-map from
the two has been combined, such that the signals that are green in either of the
states are green in the new state. The train-map is the one from the ﬁrst list
state with the location of the moving trains from the second state being added.
And the switch-rail-map is also the one from the ﬁrst state, updated such that
all switch-rails on the paths for the trains moving in the second list state ﬁt
what it in this state. This new state is then added to the resulting solution list
containing the unmergable states. Then the next state is picked from each list
and the same is done again. This continues until one of the two lists are empty.
If both run out of states at the same time, everything is good, we neither have
more states that we wanted to merge into other states or states that could be
merged into. If the ﬁrst list is not empty but the other is, we duplicate the
45
==PAGE:47==
last merged state and put them into the second list such that there is an equal
amount in both lists. We then merge the ones from the ﬁrst list into these. That
way we ensure no train is suddenly teleported backwards, which would happen
if we just put the states from the ﬁrst list into the resulting solution list. Is it
the other way round, where the second list has more elements, when the ﬁrst is
empty, we just add these to the result list as this would be where they should
go. We now have a result list of the two ﬁrst lists merged together, so next is
to take the third state list and try to merge into this result list, the same way
as before. This is continued until we have no more state lists we want to merge.
The code for this can be seen in Figure 5.3.
Figure 5.3: Code snippet from the PostProcess.fs ﬁle the Merge and Merging
functions. Used to merge lists of states together.
So now we have a state list where we have merge states together such that,
whenever a trains path is free it will be opened up. But the question is, is
this a correct solution? We know that the base used for the solution is correct,
because this was discussed before that the MAv1 does indeed ﬁnd a correct
solution. And we can used the same arguments to convinced ourselves that this
solution is also a correct solution. So we know that the ﬁrst couple states in
the solution are correct, these are the un-merged states and because the base
46
==PAGE:48==
was correct these must also be correct. Since nothing have been done except
turning signals to red after a train has passed its location, which as mentioned
does not make it non-correct. Lets now look at one of the merged states. Here
the Conductor now have the option to move more trains than before, meaning
that the game graph could branch more here. However none of the states would
lead to anything other than the goal state. This is because in the merged state
the paths have only been opened up because they were free, meaning that this
train will not be able to crash into other trains, and will at some point always
end up in goal, because it has no other options. This is the case for all the
states, no matter in which order the trains are moving they only have one path,
the one that leads to goal. And they cannot reach other trains, as the path
would not have been opened if the case. So even if the Conductor should decide
not to move the trains in the most optimal way, we know that the only option
for all the trains are to at some point end in their goal location, and doing so
without crashing into other trains. Of cause if the Conductor should decide to
move the train in another way, the Controller would also have create some other
states, so this is not an exact control program. But we can just think about
the solution as a sort of guideline for the control program. Such that when all
trains of a higher priority than some train have reached or maybe passed certain
locations, the path of this train would be opened up, and the solution found
is just the most optimal way to move the trains. And as mentioned for the
multi agent solutions, we do try to ﬁnd the most eﬃcient solution, by having
the two players play together, but still having a correct solution. This is the
Multi Agent version 2 (MAv2).
The biggest problem with this solution is that, trains might wait an unnec-
essarily long time to move. Lets say that the only location on a path that a
train has in common with another train is the second to last location. Then
there should be no reason for the train to wait at its starting location for the
other train to have passed the common location. It should be able to move to
the closest stopping point before that location. Because we know none of the
locations before this one collide with other trains, as only the second to last was
a common one, and are therefore free to be used. However this is not possible
to do, when we open up the entire path for each train for it to move. Because
this would allow the Conductor to crash the trains, as it would not have to stop
at that location, so we will need to use another solution as the base to do this.
Multi Agent Version 3
As mentioned opening up the entire path for the trains makes it impossible
to move the trains closer to the shared locations before the other trains have
passed them. However we do have a single agent solver, which only opens up a
small part of the path each Controller turn. If we use the SAv2 solution as the
basis we might be able to get around this problem. Because then we no longer
needs to ensure the entire path is free but only the next rail section of the path.
Here a section should be understood as the part of the path from the current
47
==PAGE:49==
signal to the next. So the idea for this multi agent solution is almost the same
as the other, with the exception that the paths are spilt up more, which should
allow for moving train close the problematic locations, instead of having to wait
at the start.
The way to do this is also very similar, but with some small minor changes.
Again the ﬁrst step is turning the signals to red once a train has passed the
location, this is done such that the merging might be able to happen at an earlier
state than otherwise. Again we are trying to ﬁnd the most eﬃcient solution, so
combining these will help even though it is not possible in the game. Here the
same function as for MAv2 is used. The next step is also as with the MAv2, we
are going to split the solution list into smaller lists each containing the states of
a single train moving. Here we however cannot use the same function as before,
where we just needed to check if the signal-maps changed between states, as
this is done multiple time moving a single train from start to goal this time.
So instead we need to go through the states checking from one to the next if
any trains have changed locations in the train-maps. But this is not enough
cause then we would split it at each Controller turn as well, due to the train
not changing location. So we also need to check if the signal-map has changed
and if the change is at the location of the train, that was moving in the previous
state. So we go through the solution list and ﬁnd all the splitting points, and
end up with a number of state lists each containing the states of a single train
moving. The code can be seen in Figure 5.4
Figure 5.4: Code snippet from the PostProcess.fs ﬁle the GetMovingTrains,
GetMovingTrainsStates and SplitStateListSingleSignal functions. Used to split
a state list into smaller lists of single trains moving.
The next step is the merging of states. Here the process is very similar to
MAv2 as well. We pick the ﬁrst two lists, which contain the states with the
two highest priority trains moving. The second list is the one that should be
merged into the ﬁrst. But instead of trying to ﬁnd the point where the entire
path for the second list train is free, we now look only for a point were the next
48
==PAGE:50==
section of the path is free. So ﬁrst we ﬁnd the state in the second list containing
the train using the next part of its path, and store and these locations. Then
we look through the ﬁrst list to see from which state, none of those locations
are needed, and then we merge them together like with the other version. We
now have three state lists, the resulting merged state list, the rest of the ﬁrst
list, which did not have anything merge into it, and the rest of the second list,
which is not merge into the other list yet. Here depending on what remains in
each list, not the result list with the merge states, something diﬀerent happens.
If both are empty is means we have merge all the parts where the train from
the second list moves into the ﬁrst list, and that it ﬁt perfectly with the length
of the ﬁrst list. In this case we look at what train is moving in the next state
list, with the now third highest priority train moving. We then create the state
list we want to merge this list into by picking the section of the result state list
in which the train from the next state list does not move. It seems obvious that
this should be all the state in the result list, but this is for when we reach the
states from the second pass of the game, where some of the train might move
again to avoid. So picking the states from the result list where these do not
move makes sense. When we have this we call the function again, now with this
new states list as the one to be merge into and the third of the original list to
merge into it.
If it is the case that the ﬁrst list is empty but the second is not, we just add
the remaining states from the second to the result state list. And then again in
the same way create the new list we want to merge into by picking it from the
result list, and calling the function again.
If the second list if empty but the ﬁrst is not, we are going to do as we did
with MAv2, where we pick the last merged state, and merge it into each of
the remaining states in the ﬁrst list. And again before recursively calling the
function create the new list we want to merge into.
Finally if both lists still have elements, which is most often the case, as we
only tried to merge a small part of the second list, we just recursively call the
function using these two lists. This then continues until all the state lists have
been merged together. Parts of the code for this can be seen in Figure 5.5.
49
==PAGE:51==
Figure 5.5: Code snippet from the PostProcess.fs ﬁle part of the MergingSin-
gleSignal function. Used to merge state lists together.
We now have a solution where the trains no longer wait at their starting
location until the entire path is free. But are allowed to move as far as possible
as soon as possible. But we again need to convince ourselves if this is also a
correct solution. We can use the same arguments as for the MAv2 solution.
The ﬁrst couple state if any, that are un-merged must be correct. Because we
based the solution on a correct solution, so if we have done nothing but turn
some signals red behind trains, this will still be correct. For the merged states,
we can also see that these are indeed correct without having to check each and
every Conductor edge of the game graph. This is because when a state has been
merged into another we know that the train in the merging state cannot reach
any other trains or locations which will later be used by another train with
higher priority, as it would not have been merged if that was the case. And the
train will only be allowed to move on a small section of its path, as this was the
only part that was opened up. This means that even if the order of moving the
trains should be diﬀerent than the one in the solution, the trains will just be
stopped until the other trains have reach a certain location. Such that they will
never be able to reach the same location at the same time and the train will,
as with all the solutions, only ever have one way to go, meaning that it will at
some point reach the goal location. So even if the Conductor should change the
50
==PAGE:52==
order of moving the trains, they will just be blocked at certain locations until it
is safe for them to move to the next, and eventually to goal. This is the Multi
Agent version 3 (MAv3).
As with the other solution from MAv2, the way to look at it from a game
perspective is to see it as the Controller would turn the signal green for a train,
when the trains of a higher priority have reach a certain location. So whereas
the single agent solution one could see it as when the trains are in exactly
this position turn this signal green or red. The multi agent solution is more a
guideline as to when to change signals, by looking at when all the trains of a
certain minimum priority have reach or passed these locations certain signals
can be turned green, or will be turned red. And the same for the switch-rails.
As with the other multi agent solution, the found solution is only one of
the solutions and the most optimal based on the single agent solution used as a
base. The reason these are interesting to ﬁnd at look at, is that they would be
the ones that would be used in a real life scenario. Because here we would not
have the train conductors trying to move in a way such that the trains would
crash, but instead have them move in to most eﬃcient way possible. But the
reason we are looking at a case where the conductors do wish this, is just to
ensure that if something should happen, and the trains did not move optimally
to the plan made, it would not result in any catastrophe, as we have ensured
that nothing can go wrong. The turning signals red when a train has passed,
is as mentioned not a legal move in the game, as the edge would both have a
signal change and a train location change. It should actually have a separate
state where the signal change was done. This is however also done to simulate
the real life scenario, and to make it more eﬃcient, where signals can of cause
be changed while trains are going, and also when viewing the solution it seems
more ﬂuid.
Saving The Solution
Now that we have found a solution, we might also want to see it in the GUI. To
do this we need to save it to a .sol ﬁle, in the way explained earlier. The way
this is done is by creating a long sequence of strings one for each state. These
string then contains the information about the state, which includes the trains
location, signals value and switch-rails value. We only need to save the location
and not which train is belongs to, if we ensure that it will always be done in the
same order, as the order is it loaded in the GUI. To do this we just pick out the
values of the train-map and create a string of them semicolon separated. The
same of then done for the signal-map and then the switch-rail-map, where the
value of true is replaced by a T and false by a F to reduce space. These are
then all added to the same string pattern using the sprintf function, which will
ensure that new lines and other tokens needed are all placed correctly. This is
then done for all the states of a solution list, and in the end all of it is written
to a ﬁle, which can be used to show the solution in the GUI. The code can
be seen in Figure 5.6. The exact control program, mapping the locations of
51
==PAGE:53==
trains to signal and switch-rail values, will not be extracted, and only the found
solutions can be seen in the GUI. But as mentioned before the solution for the
multi agent versions can be seen as guidelines as to when the control program
would change signals and switch-rails.
Figure 5.6: Code snippet from the SaveFile.fs ﬁle solutionSequence function.
Used create a sequence of string from a state list, that can then saved to a .sol
ﬁle.
52
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 6
The Graphical User
Interface
The second part of the project is a graphical user interface to view the solutions
found by the solver programs, and to easily create and edit new and existing
railways. The reasoning for creating a GUI was at ﬁrst to be able to see what
the solutions looked like in the actual railway. Instead of having to look at lists
of numbers symbolizing the location of the trains moving from state to state.
Having a viewer also makes debugging and checking solutions easier, as seeing
a train move illegally or weird, is easier than checking if all train locations are
correct by only looking at numbers.
When creating the Copenhagen railway (Figure 2.2) at ﬁrst, a grid was
used to model it. Having normal rails on the horizontal axis, and only moving
vertical through the use of switch-rails. This was because it was simple way
to look at a railway and giving a good overview without becoming to clotted.
Therefore it was decided that this should also be the basis for the GUI. However
it quickly became apparent that something more than just loading in a solution
was needed. Because how should the networks be translated from the numbers in
the ﬁles representing the network, to something that actually also looks correct
and how the railway was intended to look. Because the numbers representing
the locations, when creating railways by hand, were just given numbers from 1
to the number of locations, with no deeper meaning. So how does should the
program know exactly how it was meant to be drawn? Here there are two main
ideas.
The ﬁrst was to translate the numbers into something that would ﬁt in a grid.
By looking at the rails and saying these should be on the same row. However
doing this might not end up with the intended view of the railway, as there
are no indications in the numbers of how tall of a vertical jump a switch-rail
was doing. There would need to be more information which the user needed
to input, making the creation of networks even more time consuming, if the
railway should look exactly as wanted. Otherwise the program could just come
53
==PAGE:55==
up with some visual representation that would ﬁt, which might not be how it
was envisioned when made.
The second idea was to make a railway editor, where the user itself would
draw the railway, by clicking in the grid where the rails and other elements
should be. Then it could save the exact grid coordinates for each location instead
of just numbering them without deeper meaning. By doing this, when loading
the railways later, it would be ensured to look exactly how it was envisioned, and
not how some algorithm thought it should be. The editor idea also had some
more beneﬁt that would not make choosing between the ideas a problem. First
it would be easier and way more convenient to create the railways. Having an
editor responsible for writing the railway ﬁles would remove the possibility for
human errors when creating a railway. Which was a problem that was hit a few
times by mistyping a number or forgetting to put in a location, rail or other. And
to empathizes the neatness of the editor when creating the Copenhagen railway,
making the ﬁle took maybe 30 or more minutes carefully entering and checking
everything was correct by hand, and some errors were still made. Where as using
the editor it took less than 10 minutes, an the ﬁle was correct. Another extra
beneﬁt from having an editor is that one can easily edit a railway. If one wants
to expand on an existing railway or change up the locations of trains or signals
this can easily be done, if you have an editor. Where as doing this manually
would require you to edit the .txt ﬁle yourself, which is very error prone. And
ﬁnally when creating railways before the editor, the networks would be drawn
anyway to keep track of how it should look. So having the editor would give the
tools needed for this and automatically create the .txt ﬁle correctly. There is
however also one huge drawback to creating an editor. It has to be made, which
would probably take a lot more time than just writing an algorithm translating
the handmade railway ﬁles. However the beneﬁts of having an editor compared
to the cost of making one were too good and therefore it was decided to make
one.
For the next part of this chapter it will be explained how to use the editor
and the solution viewer, and how they were implemented. When starting the
program the user will be met by the menu seen in Figure 6.1, and can from here
select which of the parts to go to.
54
==PAGE:56==
Figure 6.1: The main menu of the GUI.
The Editor
The editor part of the GUI is used to create new or modify existing railways.
This is all done using a simple point and click system, to add the diﬀerent
elements to the railway.
How To Use It
When clicking the button ”Edit Railway” from the main menu the user is met
with a pop-up box asking to input the desired height of the network. When
entered another box pops up and asks for the width. These numbers can later
be increased or decreased within the editor (with a value of minimum 5 and
maximum 100), so this option is just to pick a good starting point to avoid
having to click too many times later.
When done with that the user is met by the editor window, which can be
seen in Figure 6.2.
55
==PAGE:57==
Figure 6.2: The GUI Editor window when ﬁrst entered. Using a height and
width of 10.
We see that the editor consists of two sections a menu to the left, and a grey
square containing black dots on the right. The right side is know as the drawing
panel, which is where the user can draw the network, utilizing the diﬀerent menu
items on the left and clicking on the black dots, which represent the possible
locations of the network.
The user now have two options, either they can start creating a new network
from scratch, or they can load in a network already stored from the selection
menu.
Lets ﬁrst take a look at creating a new network. If we look at Figure 6.3 we
see the menu for the editor, which contains all the options needed for creating
a network.
56
==PAGE:58==
Figure 6.3: Part of the GUI Editor menu.
Lets go through the options one by one. The ﬁrst is the ”Add rail(s)” button.
This is the one that is selected by default, as it is the most important one. Since
a railway is not a railway, if it does not have any rails. When selected if the user
clicks on one of the black dots or locations on the drawing panel, this location
will be highlighted. If the next click is on one of the locations immediately to
the right or left of the highlighted one, a rail will be created, see Figure 6.4.
Figure 6.4: Figure showing how to add a rail to the network. On the left a
location has been selected and highlighted, and on the right image the location
to the immediate right has been clicked, creating a rail been the two.
If the location selected after highlighting one is not to the immediate right or
left, the program will simply just highlight the new location. It is only possible
to create rails in the horizontal plane using the ”Add rail(s)” button. This is
due to trying to keep the program simple and not add to many less important
features. If the user wants rails that also change in the vertical plane, they
have to use the ”Add switch rail(s)” button. When clicking the button four
radio buttons and a drop down menu containing numbers become visible, see
Figure 6.5.
57
==PAGE:59==
Figure 6.5: The new options added, when clicking the ”Add switch rail(s)”
button.
The four radio button are labeled Left, Right, Up and Down, and are used
for selecting the direction of the switch-rail. The drop down menu containing
numbers is used for selecting the vertical height of the switch-rail. When the
wanted combination is selected and the user clicks a location on the drawing
panel a switch-rail will be drawn, having the intersecting location where the
user clicked. For an illustration see Figure 6.6.
Figure 6.6: Examples of switch-rails. There has been clicked on the highlighted
yellow location, with the following options selected;
Top left : Left, Up, 1.
Top right : Right, Up, 1.
Bottom left : Right Down, 1.
Bottom right : Left, Down, 2.
There are a few conditions that needs to be in place before the switch-rail
58
==PAGE:60==
can be made. First of all, the locations needed to create the switch-rail all has
to exist in the grid. So clicking the top left dot on the drawing panel, with
either option Left or Up selected will do nothing, as it would place part of the
switch-rail out of the grid. Second the user cannot place a switch-rail if the
horizontal rail of the switch-rail has already been place as a normal rail. This
is due to avoid having overlapping rails in the code. And ﬁnally two or more
switch-rails cannot be immediately connected to each other, meaning all the
three locations needed to place the switch-rail cannot already contain locations
that are part of another switch-rail.
The next option is the ”Add signal(s)” button, this also makes some new
buttons visible, but this time only two, see Figure 6.7.
Figure 6.7: The new visible buttons when selecting the ”Add signal(s)” option.
These buttons are used to select if a signal should be in direction of going
to the right or going to the left. When one has been selected, the user can now
place the signals, by clicking on a location in the grid, which is in someway
connected to a rail or switch-rail, the ones that are red. When doing so a small
R or L will be drawn above the location to symbolize that a signal is here for
going respectively right or left from this location, see Figure 6.8.
Figure 6.8: An example of signals have been placed on the drawing panel. L
meaning there is a signal at the location if going to the left. R for a signal if
going to the right, and a L and R for both.
Next up is the ”Add train(s)” button, this as the name suggests is used to
add trains to the railway. The way it works is by ﬁrst selecting a location by
clicking on it, and afterwards clicking on another location. The location picked
ﬁrst, is the starting location of the train, and the second the goal of the train.
This is shown on the drawing panel, with a uppercase T followed by a number,
written below the starting location of the train and a lowercase t followed by
the same number written below the end location see Figure 6.9. As with the
signals it is only possible to add trains to locations, that are part of a rail or
switch-rail.
59
==PAGE:61==
Figure 6.9: An example of a train having been placed, starting location at
location with T0 written below, and goal location with location written t0 below.
The next four buttons do as suggested by their names remove elements from
the railway. As with the rest of the options the desired button needs to be
selected at ﬁrst. To remove a rail the user needs to select the two end points of
the rail, so as with adding rails the same has to be done to remove them. To
remove a switch-rail the intersecting location has to be clicked. Removing a rail
or switch-rail which also contains a signal or train, will also remove the signal
or train, such that the rules for trains and signals are still in order. To remove a
signal click on the location for the signal, this will remove any signal, meaning
if the user only wanted to remove one of the two, the one to be kept has to
be placed again. As for removing trains the user has to click on the starting
location of the train. The ﬁnal button in the top part of the menu as seen in
Figure 6.3, is the ”Clear” button. Clicking this will clear the drawing panel,
meaning it will remove any rails, switch-rails, signals or trains on the drawing
panel. This button should be carefully used as there is no way to undo the
action of clearing the panel.
Moving down in the menu we now get to the middle part, which can be seen
in Figure 6.10. This consists of two normal buttons and four radio buttons.
Clicking one of the buttons will either increase the size of the grid or decrease
it, depending on the button pushed. The radio buttons are used to select in
which direction this change is gonna appear.
Figure 6.10: The middle part of the editor menu. Used for increasing or de-
creasing the size of the drawing grid, in the direction selected by the radio
buttons.
Increasing the grid size will add a column or row, to the top, bottom left or
right, depending of the selected direction. Decreasing it will remove a row or
column in the same manner. It is only allowed to decrease the size if the row or
column does not contain any rails. It is also only allowed to increase or decrease
60
==PAGE:62==
the size such that the width and height are at least 5 and at most 100.
The ﬁnal part of the menu, which can be seen in Figure 6.11, contain the
buttons to load and save a railway or return to the main menu.
Figure 6.11: The bottom part of the editor menu. Here the user can load a
stored railway or save the newly made or edited railway shown on the drawing
panel. The red box marks the area at which a message will display, letting the
user know how things went trying to save or load.
To load a railway the user has to select one from the drop down menu and
click the load button. This will override whatever is currently drawn on the
drawing panel, and replace it with the railway stored behind the selected name.
To save a railway the user need to enter a name for the railway, in the text-ﬁeld
and then click the save button. Doing this will not only save the railway, but
also run the solving program and if ﬁnding a solution for the railway save that as
well. If a solution is not found the railway itself will still be saved. When given
a name to the railway it needs to be unique, meaning if another railway stored
already has the name written in the text-ﬁeld the program will not override it,
but instead notify the user that another railway already has that name. Clicking
this button will also check if the railway follows the rules speciﬁed earlier in the
report, such as signals being placed at starting locations of the trains.
The Solution Viewer
The solution viewer is used to show the solutions found by the solver programs.
This can be done automatically or by going through the states one by one. It
is used to give a good overview of how the solution works, and make it easier
to see if something is not working as intended.
How To Use It
When clicking the ”Show solution” button from the main menu the window
seen in Figure 6.12 will pop up. From here the user will be able to select any
61
==PAGE:63==
stored network, which also has a corresponding solution ﬁle from the drop down
menu. The grey box on the right with all the black dots is just a placeholder
until a railway has been loaded.
Figure 6.12: The Solution Viewer window, when ﬁrst opened.
When the user has selected a railway they can click the ”Load solution”
button. Doing so will load in the railway, and the solution to the railway, and
make some more buttons and other info visible on the left menu panel, see
Figure 6.13.
62
==PAGE:64==
Figure 6.13: The Solution Viewer window, after loading a railway and solution.
Here showcasing the Copenhagen railway, solved using MAv3.
If we start by looking at the drawing panel on the right, it will be noticed
that the representation of trains, signals and switch-rails to some degree have
been modiﬁed from the editors drawing panel. The trains are now symbolized
by a image of a train, located at the current position of the train, and moving
in the direction of the image. The signals have been replaced with small black
boxes containing either a red or a green circle, representing the signal being red
or green, just above the locations. The location of the black box represents the
direction of the signal. If the box is to the right of the location, it is a signal
that is used by trains moving to the right from the location, and vice versa for
the boxes on the left of a location. Finally a slight alteration has been made
to the switch-rails. It is now displayed which of the rails is selected, which is
the one drawn in red, whereas the the on in black is the other option, which
is not usable in the current state. For at closer look at the railway objects see
Figure 6.14.
63
==PAGE:65==
Figure 6.14: We here see the new models being drawn for the railway objects.
A train with the direction right. Located at the same location of a green light
for trains going right. A red signal on the location just to the right, also for
right moving trains, and a red signal for left moving trains to the location right
of that. And a switch-rail where the horizontal rail is currently selected.
If we now move our attention to the left panel, we can see that we now
have more options, Figure 6.15. This is showing the user information about the
solution, and will allow the user to see the solution played out, or go through it
state by state.
Figure 6.15: The menu opening up when loading a railway and solution. So-
lution length is the number of states in the solution. Generated states is the
number of states generated by the solver program when ﬁnding the solution.
Time spend is the total time spend to ﬁnd the solution by the solver program.
Start button is to start the showing of the solution, stop to stop it, and restart
to stop and return to the ﬁrst state. The next state and previous state button
are for moving one state at a time.
64
==PAGE:66==
To view the solution the user will only need to click the ”Start” button,
this will automatically then show each state in the solution in order on the
drawing panel to the right. Starting from the one currently drawn state, and
stopping when there are no more states in the solution.
To pause/stop the
automatic showing the user can press the ”Stop” button or ”Restart” button.
Both will stop it, with the ﬁrst then pausing it at the current state and the
second resetting it to the ﬁrst state in the solution. If the user want to move
more slowly through the solution, the two buttons on the bottom can be used.
By clicking the ”Next state” button the state after the one currently on the
right will be drawn, and the ”Previous state” will draw the state before the
current. These are very useful for checking that everything looks correct when
moving from one state to another, or narrow down something that looks wrong.
Both of these buttons of cause only works if there is a ”next” or ”previous”
state in the solution, otherwise nothing will happen when clicking them. If the
user so wishes, they can at anytime load another solution, by selecting another
railway and clicking the ”Load solution” button again. This will load in the
new railway and showcase the solution for that, always drawing the ﬁrst state
of the solution.
Implementation
Java is used as the programming language for the GUI, so the ﬁrst thing that
needs to be done is to create the F# types but now as Java classes. These
are pretty straight forward, where each class contains a ﬁeld for each element
needed for that type. With the exception of trains, which now also contains
the start and end locations and direction. Locations has not been made into a
class as this is just an integer. As mentioned there are two parts to the GUI the
editor and solution viewer. To ensure that the railways drawn in the editor will
look the same in the solution viewer, some general classes has been made for
the menu panel and drawing panel, which the speciﬁc panels for the editor and
solution viewer will extend. These set up the the basics of the panels such as
size and colour. The general drawing panel is also responsible for drawing all of
the railway elements for the editor. Some of these functions are overridden by
the solution drawing panel, such that we get the look that we want. The reason
that it has not been moved to the editor drawing panel class, is because some of
the elements are drawn the same for both panels, such as the rails for instance.
So it serves as a basis for drawing railways. With the exception of these classes
being used as a base for both parts, the rest are rather independent so lets look
at them individually. Starting with the editor.
To model the railway networks six data-structures are used. Two integer
matrices and four lists. One of the matrices is used for the rails and the other
for the signals. The lists are used for the rails, switch-rails, signals and trains.
The reason to have both a list and a matrix for some of the elements, it that it is
faster and easier to look up in a matrix than to run through all the elements of
a list, when checking if the user input can be done. This will be explained more
65
==PAGE:67==
later. The editor consists of two panels a menu panel on the left and a drawing
panel on the right. Everything is set to have a ﬁxed size. This is just to avoid
having to dynamically adjust everything, and avoid having things look weird,
if the user should decide to change the window size to a weird scaling. On the
menu panel a lot of buttons are placed, all the ones above the ”Clear” button
are used to set ﬂags in the code. Such that when the user clicks on the drawing
panel the desired action happens. These are not really that interesting to talk
about. The ”Clear” button, will just remove everything on the drawing panel,
which is done by clearing the data-structures used to keep track of the drawn
railway. The next set of buttons are the ”Decrease” and ”Increase” button,
which are used for changing the size of the drawing grid. The direction button
are used to set a ﬂag such that the size change happens in the correct place.
These two button are rather interesting, as changing the grid size is not just
adding or removing a row or column. Because the way the locations are stored
and created is that the location value is calculated as follows i × W + j, where
i is the row in the grid, W is the width of the grid and j is the column in the
grid. Doing it this way all grid locations will have a unique number between
0 and width times height minus one. This however means that when we want
to change the grid size, we need to go through all the elements already placed
to ensure that the locations they use get updated correctly.
Otherwise the
railway will not look at intended, as the locations then would be placed weirdly
in the grid. So for instance if we want to add a row to the top of the grid
every location needs to have the width of the grid added to their value to ﬁt
correctly into the updated grid. This is done for each direction, ensuring that
when adding a row or column, or removing a row or column everything looks
like it should afterwards. The code for adding a row on top of the grid can be
seen in Figure 6.16.
66
==PAGE:68==
Figure 6.16: Code snippet from the DrawingPanel.java ﬁle part of the increas-
eSize function. Used to add an empty row to the top of the drawing grid.
Next is the drop-down menu of the stored railways.
This is created by
looking in the ”Railways” folder for any ﬁle with the extension .txt, and the
ﬁlename will be added to this menu. When one is selected and the ”Load”
button is clicked, it will override whatever is on the drawing panel and draw the
railway from the ﬁle. The ﬁle is loaded in much the same way as done in the
F# program, where each line in gone through loading in and converting from
strings to the data-types needed. When done all of the data-structures, which
has the information of the currently drawn railway, will be replaced with the
data just loaded. As mentioned these data-structures not only contains lists of
the types, but also two matrices. These also need to be updated in the correct
manner, so lets explain how they work. The signal matrix is the simple of the
two. The matrix has the same dimensions as the grid, such that when a signal is
67
==PAGE:69==
placed in the grid a number is added to the corresponding place in the matrix.
Each grid point represents one matrix index in a direct translation, such that
top left of grid is top left of matrix. If the signal is a left directional signal a 1
is place in the matrix, if its a right directional signal a 2 is placed, and if both
are placed a 3 is added to the matrix. As mention the matrix is used such that
we do not have to loop through the signal list each time to check if a signal is
already present if the user should try to add multiple of the same.
The other matrix the rail matrix is a bit more complex. This also contains
speciﬁc values depending on what kind of rail connected to a location. Again
the matrix has the same dimensions as the grid, such that each location in the
grid has a corresponding index in the matrix. For normal rails the two locations
which it is connecting will have either a 1 or a 2 value in the grid. 1 means
that it is at an end rail, so the location is only connected to one rail, and a
2 means that the location is also connected to another rail. Swicth-rails are
also represented in the matrix using the numbers 3 through 6. 3 meaning that
the location is the intersecting location of a switch-rail, 4 is the end of the rail
on the same row as the intersecting location. Then we have 5 and 6. These
are both used for the last location that is part of the switch-rail, but the value
depend on the direction of the switch-rail. If the direction is left then 5 is used
otherwise 6. The reason for all these speciﬁc numbers is to ensure that the rules
of the railways cannot be broken, if the user tries to place elements wrong. For
instance not allowing placing a switch-rail next to another, can easily be checked
by looking in this matrix for a 4, 5 or 6, at any of the needed locations. This
matrix is also a map over where the locations of the railway are, which means
that when trying to place a signal or train, we only need to check this matrix
to check if possible. The alternative is too loop through the rail and switch-rail
lists, which could be very time consuming for large railways. These matrices
are ﬁlled by looping through the loaded data, and adding the correct values in
the correct indices. As mentioned the railways are stored in a folder, the path
for this folder is currently setup as a MacOS path, and will need to be changed
for other operating systems.
The ﬁnal elements in the menu are the text-ﬁeld and the ”Save” button. The
input in the text-ﬁeld will be the name used when saving the railway. However
saving is not the only thing the button does, because it will also try to solve
the network when saved. So lets ﬁrst look at how the saving is done, and then
the solving. The ﬁrst thing it does when the button is pressed is to see if the
railway is legal, meaning it follows the signal rules stated earlier. Such as all
starting locations of trains needing a signal in the same direction as the train.
It will then create a new .txt ﬁle using the input in the text-ﬁeld as the name.
But only if no other ﬁle has that name in the Railways folder. The reason for
not creating the ﬁle if another has the same name is to avoid risking to override
another railway. If we remember the railway ﬁle setup, we know that the ﬁrst
thing that it contains is the locations.
These are found by looping through
the rail and switch-rail lists, adding all locations from these to a set, to avoid
duplicates. The set is then converted to a list which is then sorted, and the
locations are one by one written to the ﬁle each on a separate line. When no
68
==PAGE:70==
more locations are in the list, the type separating colon is added on a separate
line. The next type in the ﬁle is the rails. Here we sort the rail list and add
them again one by one with the locations they connect being written, separated
by a space, and when done adding the colon line. The same then happens for
the switch-rails and the signals, each written such that the information in the
ﬁle is correct. Sorting the lists is technically not necessary but make the ﬁles
look less messy. When reaching the train list, we do not sort, because the order
of the list is already the order of the trains. As the train class does not have a id
ﬁeld, we just keep a counter when going through the list updating it whenever
a train has been written to the ﬁle, and using this for the id. Finally the width
and height of the grid is also written to the ﬁle. Now that the railway network
has been saved, we can try to ﬁnd a solution for it. The way this is done is
by running a command-line command, which will execute the solver program
on a speciﬁed ﬁle and storing the solution in a speciﬁed directory. To do this
we ﬁrst need to have a compiled the F# solver program, which is easily made
into an executable. As this is a F# executable to make this run on MacOS the
Mono program [7] has to be used. Doing this on other operating systems might
therefore require a slight modiﬁcation to the command used. The executable
takes in two command line arguments the ﬁlename and directory of the ﬁle, and
will then run the solver program. If the program succeeds the solution is stored
at the speciﬁed directory, if not the user will be notiﬁed via a text-ﬁeld update
in the menu.
As the solver might run for a while before ﬁnding a solution,
especially if the ﬁrst single agent version is used, the GUI will just wait until it
is done. The code to do this can be seen in Figure 6.17.
Figure 6.17: Code snippet from the SaveButtonListener.java ﬁle part of the
actionPerformed function. The command use to run the solver program from
the command-line.
The second part of the editor is the drawing panel. As mentioned the drawing
of the railway happens in the general drawing panel class. What it does is that
it loops through all of the lists and matrices and draws the elements at the
speciﬁed locations. The class however have one important role, which is to add
elements to the lists. This is done using a mouse listener, which when clicked will
check the ﬂags set by the diﬀerent menu buttons and call the correct function.
69
==PAGE:71==
If the ﬂags are set to an element which require multi clicks a highlight will be
drawn on the ﬁrst click, which also ensures that the location will be remembered
for the next click. Lets go through them one by one. Adding a rail requires two
clicks, on two locations next to each other on the same row. But a second rail
can be added using only a third click, if it is next to the second click. When
trying to add a rail it will be checked using the rail matrix whether or not it is
possible, such that the railway will not break any rules. The same is the case for
the switch-rails, this only requires one click, but will check all three locations
in the rail matrix to see if possible or not. If not possible nothing will happen
otherwise the rails will be added, and a repaint function call is performed on
the panel to make it appear on the screen. Adding signals will only require
to click on the location, at which is should be placed, and it will be placed as
long as a rail is present. Finally placing a train requires that the user clicks on
the start location ﬁrst and then the goal, both of which needs to have a rail
connection somehow. It is not checked by the editor that the starting location
and goal location of a train are connected, but when trying to ﬁnd a solution
it will quickly return saying nothing was found. Removing things except the
normal rails only requires one click on the signal, starting location of a train or
the intersecting location of a switch-rail. Doing this will update the necessary
lists and matrices. Finally removing a rail requires clicks on both ends of the
rails. So the drawing panel is basically responsible for checking if the user clicks
what ﬂags are set and perform the correct action if possible.
The solution viewer as mentioned also consists of a menu panel and a drawing
panel. Initially the menu panel only consists of a drop down menu, a ”Load”
button and a ”Back” button. The drop down menu lists all the names of ﬁles in
the Railways folder which both has a .txt and .sol ﬁle. We need both because
the .txt ﬁle knows the layout of the railway, while the .sol ﬁle knows the states in
the solution. When a railway has been selected and the ”Load” button pressed,
the railway will be loaded in the same manner as for the editor. The solution
will also be loaded but in a diﬀerent way. The way the solution is loaded is
through the use of regular expressions to ﬁnd the ﬁrst line which contains the
train locations in the ﬁrst state of the solution. We then know that the next
line in the ﬁle is the signals and the ﬁnal line is the switch-rails. Each of these
line are saved as the string from the ﬁle, before we scrape them by removing
the start token, square brackets and remove blank spaces. We then split each
string by semicolons, such that we know have a list of the elements. The string
elements are then converted to the correct data-types, and added to lists. The
train locations to integers and the F and Ts to false and true boolean values.
We now have a list of locations, a list of signal values and a list of switch-rail
values corresponding to the ﬁrst state. These are then converted to maps, and
each map added to a list. This is then done again for the next state in the ﬁle,
and continues until there are no more states. In the end we will have three lists
of maps, such that the ﬁrst element in each list contains the maps for the ﬁrst
state and the second for the second and so on. The end of the solution ﬁle also
contains some more data such as time spent solving and number of generated
states, which in a similar fashion is retrieved. The code for the state retrieval
70
==PAGE:72==
can be seen in Figure 6.18.
Figure 6.18: Code snippet from the RailwayLoader.java ﬁle part of the loadSo-
lution function. Used to load data from .sol ﬁles.
When loaded the railway will be displayed in the drawing panel, where the
location of the trains are based on the ﬁrst state, which means that ﬁrst element
in the train-map list. The signals will be based on the signal-map, being red if
false and green if true, and the switch-rails allowing the rail on the same row to
be used if true, and the other rail if false. The trains and signals are also being
drawn diﬀerently than in the editor, with the trains being a small image of a
train, and the signal being a black square with either a red or green circle. These
are being drawn such that the size ﬁts the grid and will automatically have the
best size for the drawn network. The switch-rails are also slightly changed, with
the selected rail being red as the normal rails and the non-selected rail being
drawn in black. To view the solution the start button has to be pressed. This
will start a timer, which every 100 milliseconds will update a counter used to
keep track of which element in the map lists is being drawn. That way every
100 milliseconds the state being drawn will be updated to the next state in the
list, and the solution will play out. The stop button will stop this timer and
71
==PAGE:73==
the last state drawn, when clicked will be the one shown on the drawing panel.
The restart button will reset the counter to 0, such that the ﬁrst state is drawn
again. This will also stop the timer. The last two buttons are the next and
previous state buttons, which will stop the timer, and manually can be used to
increase or decrease the counter, to move carefully move through the states.
72
==PAGE:74==
Chapter 7
Results
In this chapter the results from testing the six solvers on seven railway networks
will be shown. First the result will be stated in a table for each solver, and
afterward the results for each network will be displayed in a table of its own.
The seven networks used for the testing are the railway networks, which can
be seen in the appendix. The networks have been chosen to showcase strength
and weaknesses of the solvers. The columns in the tables for the solver has an
abbreviation for what it contains. These will now be explained. Pre. means
that time spent in preprocessing. Sol. means the time spent in the game. Post.
is time spent in post-processing, this is only relevant for the last two multi agent
solvers. Tot. is the total time spend ﬁnding a solution. So the sum of the ﬁrst
three numbers. Length is the length of the found solution, and Gen. is the
number of states that were generated during the game. All times are measured
in millisecond. If a ﬁeld is left blank in a table, it means that no solution was
found by that solver in a reasonable time, which was set at ﬁve minutes. All
the timings are taken as an average of running the program on the network ten
times. As multiple of the solutions use the same preprocessing, all but the ﬁrst,
and some of the multi agent version use solutions found by the single agent
solvers, these numbers will be equal in the tables. All of the solution ﬁles for
the networks are not included in the report, but can be found with the source
code. The experiments have been made on a MacBook Pro with a 2,3 GHz
Dual-Core Intel Core i5 processor and 8GB of RAM.

In the result tables for each network, only the total time spent, solution
length and generated states will be shown. This is because these are the most
interesting metrics. Along with each solver another row has been added to table.
This contains the worst case solution length, meaning the longest solution, which
could exist for the network. This has been calculated as the sum of the distances
from the starting location to the goal location of all train, plus the number of
signals, on the path for each train with the most signals. If a solution exists, that
uses exactly these paths, is unsure, but we know that no other solution could
have a longer state list, and therefore this is chosen as the worst case. It has not
been possible to calculate the best case solution in a similar fashion. One could
think that it must be the longest of the shortest paths for the trains. Which is
a lower limit. However it is not that simple. Just using the path length, might
correspond to a solution where multiple train have to use the same location at
the same time, or at least being a non-correct solution, meaning that it is not
possible. So calculating the best case is not that simple and no smart way other
than brute forcing the solution exists.
The Lyngby network, is a small and compact network, where the trains are
part of two separate swap-cycles. This network is chosen for being a small and
not to simple network.

Chapter 8
Discussion
In this chapter we will discuss the results from running the solver on the selected
railway networks. As we can see from the results, the SAv1 clearly has the
problems stated earlier, that when the number of trains go up or the solution
requires a very speciﬁc order of moving the trains is cannot ﬁnd a solution. This
is likely due to the high branching factor for the Controller, as it can generate
up to two times the number of trains new states each turn. The Copenhagen
and CopenhagenReal networks have the same amount of trains, but the later of
the two was not solved in time. This is due to the way the trains need to move
in order to ﬁnd a solution. If the Controller goes and places the T5 too early,
it will have to backtrack a lot, since this could end up blocking other trains. If
we look at the LineSwapping networks, we can also clearly see this pointed out
again. For the normal small one with just four trains, and very little choice,
only containing two switch-rails, and the trains are all on a row, it generates 939
states for a 57 state solution. In the more open networks it generates around 3
times the states as it has in the solution, but when the path needed to be taken
is too speciﬁc, as it is in this case, it clearly lacks some guidance. We can also see
that it is the fastest to ﬁnd a solution in the small Lyngby network and ZigZag
network, due to not having that much preprocessing time. But as soon as the
networks get a little more complicated or bigger, the other single agent solvers
make up for the extra time spent in preprocessing by ﬁnding a solution much
faster, and in some cases ﬁnding a solution, when this version could not. So it
is clearly time well spend in preprocessing as it not only allow the solutions to
be found faster, but also allows solutions to be found which could not be found
without.
If we now move the attention to the other two single agent solvers the SAv2
and SAv3, we can see that the SAv3 is a bit better as the solutions are found
faster and in less states. This is due to the fact that the Controller only has very
few states it generates, whereas it in the SAv2 generates many more, at least
one for each signal the trains have to pass. We can also see that the solution
found by the SAv2 are often very close to or actually equal to the worst case
solution. This is not necessarily a bad thing, since the worst case is often close
79
==PAGE:81==
to the best case for a single agent only solution. But as is the case for both
of these versions the number of generated states are very close to, and for the
SAv3 always equal to, the length of the solution. For the SAv3 this means that
when we simulate the game, we always only generate the states needed to get to
a solution. We see that the branching value for the graph generation is down to
one, which means that when searching we do not really search as much as we just
go the correct way. This is due to the priorities and train paths found during
the preprocessing, which allows the Controller to only generate one state, which
will be part of the solution. There might be some networks this can not solve.
Such as networks that are closer to being a puzzle than a railway, or containing
some edge cases, which are not covered in the current implementation. But
for the ones shown here and many other networks, it was always the case that
no states, except the ones part of the solution, were generated by the SAv3.
A problem this might have however, which has not been tested, is when the
networks get very large. Here the preprocessing might start taking a while to
do. But when done, the game should be solved very fast and eﬃcient, as the
branching factor in the game is one and therefore ﬁnding a solution should not
take much time.
For the ﬁrst of the multi agent solvers, MAv1, we can see it spends roughly
the same time as the SAv3, which makes sense as they are very similar. It also
as with the SAv3, only generates the states needed in the solution, and usually
fewer as it allows for multiple trains to move at the same time. However as men-
tioned this version has one big problem, which can be seen in the LineSwapping
networks. If the paths for the trains are all overlapping this is just a single agent
solver. So this means that the multi agent part is based on network it is trying
to solve, which is not optimal, as these networks clearly do have multi agent
solutions. However if a network is set up in such a way that all of the train have
non-crossing paths, this will ﬁnd the optimal solution, which is just open all of
the paths at the same time. So this version of the solver is very dependent on
the network it is solving, and will only be multi agent on some.
Something similar can be said about the MAv2. We can see that in the
networks, where the overlapping part of the trains paths are very small, like
Lyngby and ZigZag, this solution is very good.
A lot of states are merged
and the solution length reduce but a lot. But when the networks are very tight,
understood as the trains paths are overlapping a lot, such as LongLineSwapping
there is close zero multi agent movement happening. This is of cause due to
the fact that the entire path has to be free for the trains to move. So when
other trains are also needing to use a lot of the same locations, there is very
little merging to be done. Comparing this to the MAv1 we see that it is always
better, as it should be since, it is based on merging the MAv1 solution. And it
only requires a very little extra time to do so. If we look at this from a real life
perspective, this might be one of the most energy eﬃcient solutions that could
exists, due to the fact that the trains once started will not be stopped by red
signals until they reach their goal. Unless the train is the one asked to go to a
safe location, in which case it will be stopped once. But as mentioned it has its
problems when networks are very tight and crammed.
80
==PAGE:82==
This is not a problem for the ﬁnal multi agent solver. When looking through
the results, with the exception of Lyngby, it is clear that this is the one that
perform the best in terms of solution length. It does spend more time than
any of the other solver, if we do not count the ﬁrst single agent not ﬁnding
solutions to some network. But it also ﬁnds the shortest solution of them all. It
is believed that the solution it ﬁnds is very close to a best case solution, when the
solution also has to be correct. This is due to the fact that the trains are allowed
to more as soon as possible when it is safe. But as mentioned calculating the
length of a best case solution is not easy, so cannot say for sure. If we look at the
results from the LongLineSwapping, which the MAv2 has some problems with,
we can see this is not the case for this version. Here the states can be merged
together and being a tight network is not a problem. There are however some
networks where this would not work as well as the other multi agent solvers.
These are networks with close to no overlapping or non-overlapping paths for
all the trains and with many signals. Here the other solution would most likely
perform better, as the number signal changes would mean, that this solution
has to initially generate many more states. This would lead to a ﬁnal solution
being longer than for the ones, that just opens the entire paths for the trains.
So perhaps if one could combine the two solvers MAv2 and MAv3 in some
way, a smart and better solution could be found, as they each have a weakness
that the other does not. As mentioned this performs the best when looking at
solution length, but if we look at the time spent, we can see that this is the one
needing the most time. This is because the merging of states for this is a bit
more complicated that for the MAv2. Here we look at many more small state
sequences, that should be merged together, which results in using more time.
This can clearly be seen in the Copenhagen10Swap network, where it is multiple
times slower than the other. But the found solution is also much shorter.
81
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 9
Future Works
If continuing to work on the project there are a few directions that would be
interesting to take. First up it would be interesting to try to use the ideas from
merging the solutions and the single agent solvers to try to create an actual
multi agent solver, to see how it would compare to the single agent merged
solutions.
The next thing that could be interesting, is to add more elements to the
domain to make it more realistic. As mentioned this is a simpliﬁed model of a
real railway, so adding more things like diﬀerent kinds of switch-rails or signals,
allowing rails to cross or one-way rails to mention a few. This would open up
some other interesting scenarios which would require some more things to keep
track of. Following the realism idea it could also be interesting to add a time
perspective.
Meaning that the trains will also be given a time it has to be
somewhere, maybe even give them multiple locations with timestamps. Doing
this it would change it to a train scheduling problem and it could be interesting
to see if the ideas explored in this project, could also be used to eﬃciently solve
that problem.
Doing this would also require the trains to have speeds, and
distances of the rails, which have been completely ignored now, and assumed
to all be constantly the same. This could also open up for the idea of diﬀerent
kinds of trains, some which might go faster than others and some that might
occupy multiple locations.
One of the problems with the GUI is that it is limited by the size. This
means that the railways that can be made are limited. So a feature that could
be nice to add is a zoom function, such that the user could zoom in when the
railway is getting too big. That way it would no longer be limited by the size
of the window and larger railways could be constructed.
Another nice feature to add to the GUI, would be a way to play the game.
As either the Controller or Conductor to show how the game works, and how
complicated it can get.
82
==PAGE:84==
Chapter 10
Conclusion
In this project a simple railway setting was explored, implementing a program
to ﬁnd a correct-by-construction control program for the railway network. This
was done by looking at the problem as a two player game, one player control-
ling the signals and switch-rails and the other the trains. That way a graph
could be created over the game. Then by searching through this graph a path
could be found, which would ensure that the trains were always guided safely
to their goals, resulting in a correct control program. The game graph exponen-
tially grows in size with the railway network size, making larger networks close
to impossible to solve generating the entire game state space. This problem
was solved by using on-the-ﬂy generating of states. This in combination with
pre-calculation of the paths the trains should take and order that the trains
should move, allowed us to reduce the number of states generated to only be
the ones needed in the solution. Meaning that no state that was not needed was
generated, making it very eﬃcient.
These solutions however only allowed for a single train to move at a time, so
if seen in a real life scenario, the solution itself might be seen as ineﬃcient, as
only moving a single train on an entire railway is often not optimal. As there is
generally space for more to move. So to also make the solution itself eﬃcient,
the states of single agent solutions were merged into multi agent solutions, which
would allow more trains to move at a time, without loosing the correctness of
the control program. Doing this reduces some of the solution length by quite a
lot, making the solutions themselves also eﬃcient.
To create the networks, and see the solutions found by the program, a graph-
ical user interface was also implemented.
This allowed for easy creation of
networks, through a simple interface, reducing the error prone method of hand-
writing the networks. While also ensuring that the requirements of the railway
domain were not broken.
The other part of the GUI allow for showing the
control program found, by playing through the solutions one state at a time.
This helped get a visual idea of how the railways where solved, at made ﬁnding
problems in a solution easier, than looking through the text based states given
by the control program.
83

small railway network, which includes problems such as limited space and trains
needing to swap locations.
